## Thread: CALL using OMITTED — 2 this month / 12 total

_Thread started [2008-09](2008-09.md#t-7d50d2697b)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: CALL using OMITTED

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-10-01T00:07:14+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6kefj4F6nsulU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<48DD0EC9.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <p5hDk.137$J63.102@newsfe08.iad> <48E118C2.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <ihhEk.97$cs4.78@newsfe01.iad>`

```


"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:ihhEk.97$cs4.78@newsfe01.iad...
> Frank Swarbrick wrote:
>>>>>On 9/26/2008 at 8:43 PM, in message <p5hDk.137$J63.102@newsfe08.iad>,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
<snip>

I thought your idea of using collections for parameters was imaginative, but 
there are a couple of problems with it, as far as I'm concerned.

1. Encapsulation.  An object instance of a Class should be encapsulated. 
PROPERTIES achieve that, sharing parameters in a Collection, doesn't. It 
becomes unwieldy when you start having a separate Collection for each 
program, although you certainly COULD do that. The problem is that the 
Collection has to be shared so that whatever is calling can set it up. That 
violates encapsulation. Using the PROPERTY approach, only the object which 
owns the property can ever access it. "Outsiders" have to do so through a 
method of the object.

2. There are a variety of ways to access the collection (indexes, keys, 
hashes, etc.) and this gets confusing. The access method needs to be 
overridden if you want to access with a key to a parameter instead of an 
index. If you manage it with collection indexes they have to be maintained 
when a parameter is deleted, and so on. It is DOable, but unwieldy in my 
opinion. (You may not be aware, but Fujitsu (at Enterprise level) offer a 
rich series of Collection Classes for COBOL. They include:

COLLECTION CLASS

CLEAR-COLLECTION Makes the current collection empty.
CLONE-COLLECTION Creates a replica of the current collection.
CONTAINS-ELEMENT Checks whether the current collection contains a specified
element.
CONTAINS-ALL-ELEMENT Checks whether the current collection contains all 
elements in
a specified collection.
CREATE-ITERATOR Creates an iterator object for the current object.
GET-SIZE Returns the number of elements in the current collection.
IS-EMPTY Checks whether the current collection is empty

LIST CLASS

CREATE-LIST-ITERATOR Creates a list iterator object for the current list.
EQUALS-LIST-COLLECTION Checks whether the current list matches a specified
one.

LINKED LIST CLASS

ADD-FIRST-ELEMENT Adds an element to the head of the current list.
ADD-LAST-ELEMENT Adds an element to the end of the current list.
GET-FIRST-ELEMENT Returns an element at the head of the current list.
GET-LAST-ELEMENT Returns an element at the end of the current list.
REMOVE-FIRST-ELEMENT Deletes an element at the head of the current list.
REMOVE-LAST-ELEMENT Deletes an element at the end of the current list.

SET CLASS

ADD-ELEMENT Adds an element to the current set.
ADD-ALL-ELEMENT Adds all the elements included in a specified
collection to the current set.
EQUALS-SET-COLLECTION Checks whether the current set and a specified set
are equivalent.
REMOVE-ELEMENT Removes an element from the current set.
REMOVE-ALL-ELEMENT Removes all the elements included in a specified
collection from the current set.

MAP CLASS

CLEAR-COLLECTION Makes the current map empty.
CLONE-COLLECTION Creates a replica of the current map.
CREATE-ITERATOR Creates an iterator object for the current map.
GET-SIZE Returns the number of entries for the current map.
IS-EMPTY Checks whether the current map is empty.

MAP ENTRY CLASS

CONTAINS-KEY Checks whether a specified key is contained in the
current map.
CONTAINS-VALUE Checks whether a specified value (object) is
contained in the current map.
EQUALS-MAP-COLLECTION Checks whether the current map matches a
specified map.
GET-VALUE Returns a value (object) of a specified key from
the current map.
PUT-ENTRY Adds an entry (key and value) to the current map.
PUT-ALL-ENTRY Adds all entries (keys and values) contained in a
specified map to the current map.
REMOVE-ENTRY Deletes an entry (key and value) of a specified key
from the current map.

COLLECTION EXCEPTION CLASS

GET-CLASS-NAME Returns the class name of a class library in which an
exception object occurs.
GET-METHOD-NAME Returns the method name of a class library in which an
exception object occurs.
GET-CODE Returns an exception type.
GET-MESSAGE Returns an exception message

I have never had occasion to use them in COBOL, but I use collections in C# 
and VB all the time. I think it is fair to consider a set of parameters to a 
program as a collection. It is the mechanics of it, not the concept which I 
don't personally like.

.
>
> Pete's sentence :-
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
> conveys the idea.

I see what you are saying, but I wouldn't do it. (Too complex...)

>
> I think you can see the germ of an idea. OO is infinitely flexible and you 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The problem is that parameters need to be associated with a Class, and 
different methods of the Class may require different parameters. I use 
Object PROPERTIES because they are owned and accessed by the Class, and 
access to them is simple and unambiguous.

Connecting a group of parameters on a file or database (or in a 
Collection...) and then using different entry points to access them is 
technically feasible, but I have never worked on a site where they would 
allow this.

I think it is an imaginative approach but it is just too complex and 
unwieldy, compared to using properties.

Just one man's opinion...:-)

Pete.
```

```
Frank,

No doubt you found the suggestions from Pete and myself interesting, but 
not particularly germane to your current problem for mainframe 
Procedural. I'll get back to Pete's comments later, but first......

Wont guarantee it, but working 'backwards, from my thoughts on 
Collections, I might just hit on a suggested Procedural solution - but 
no promises.

I need to know a few things. I know confidentiality is involved, but can 
you perhaps illustrate the sort of parameter requirements and why, 
without being specific,

"This program is being used to check dodgy customers and close their 
accounts, fr' instance Freddie and Fannie, AIG etceterah"

- so get 'clever' hide the real intent; some dummy parameter names 
without giving the game away.

So now some questions :-

- How many sub-programs being called and any need for expansion.

- Is there a maximum number of parameters covering all programs above

- Actual parameters can be permutated per sub-program - correct ?

- Parameter fields can be a mix of pic x(?) and pic 9(?) - can you 
specify a maximum size for BOTH; should you say hit on a max pic x(512), 
can you also come up with a desirable smaller average value; i.e. we 
have two options covering pic x(?) parameter sizes. (My own thoughts - 
can't see that you would be passing pix c x(512) - but unless I ask, we 
aren't going to know).

- A point I made in my original e-mail, is each set of parameters only 
applicable to a particular sub-program or can Permutation-A apply to 
sub-programs 2, 5, 7; is there a Permutation-B that would apply to 
sub-programs 1,3, 4, 6

Puhleeese - if possible illustrate your parameters at the moment and the 
sort of pic x(x) or pic 9(x) values they hold.

 From above I'll try and figure what I can do. If you can think of 
anything else pertinent to the problem that I haven't indicated, please 
let me/us know.

Jimmy
```

## Thread: My Migrations — 52 this month / 60 total

_Topics: [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

_Thread started [2008-09](2008-09.md#t-04b1bde0e3)._

##### ↳ ↳ Re: My Migrations

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-10-01T12:40:31+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad>`

```


"tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad...
>
> Lee Unterreiner <lee@db-net.nospam.com> wrote in message
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> sufficient to convert EVERYTHING.

You got that right...:-) So maybe more than one person is needed?

>You must have some starting point.  (F'r
> instance, the Prime computers are long since gone, but there is a
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> the world to a proprietary product?

Yes.

>Particularly Microsoft!

Yes.

> For all the
> virtues of their products, they owe their dominance to superior marketing
> and the bandwagon effect, rather than to technical superioirty.

I'd dispute that. We used to say the same about IBM in the old days. In this 
part of the world it's called "tall poppy syndrome", but it is based on envy 
rather than a dispassionate evaluation. 
(http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tall_poppy_syndrome)

The fact is that MicroSoft DID have some bad products and they still have 
some poor products, but .NET is NOT one of those. And the difference is that 
when you have the resources they have, you can get the best people and 
improve the poor products. Anders Hejlsberg was bought from Borland to 
provide C#, for example. I don't think he has ever regretted moving to 
MicroSoft. Now he gets to do things he could only dream about before. He has 
the resources he needs to implement dreams and we are all better off because 
of it. He is not the only one, I'm just citing him as an example.

My own experience with MS products has been one of consistent improvement. 
Vista has faults (most of them were enforced onto MS by pressure groups) but 
they are being addressed, and Windows 7 will be better. (If it isn't then 
I'll exercise an option and move to LAMP. Note that the fact that MicroSoft 
is a monopoly does not preclude me doing that...). Meantime, XP with SP3 is 
the first OS I have had on a PC that I am totally happy with. It has never 
crashed or BSODed and it does what I expect of it.

This is not to say that there is anything wrong with Linux or Unix or other 
non-MS OSes either. It is just that being anti MS because they are big, or a 
monopoly, is, well, stupid...

.NET was/is a fantastic concept and the implementation of it has borne out 
the promise. Until you actually work with it you can't really understand why 
it is important. It is a truly level playing field that allows all OO 
languages to interact seamlessly...even COBOL. Apart from that it provides 
programmers with many thousands of easy-to-use functions and components. 
However, people who can't even see the importance of Object Orientation are 
unlikely to recognize the value of such a platform. The fact that the non-MS 
camp has produced an equivalent in Mono should indicate that the concept is 
important. (Mono, BTW, is an excellent implementation. However it doesn't 
yet have InterOP services (so COBOL can't play on it, unless it is COBOL for 
.NET) or LINQ and will lag behind .NET until it does.)

C# can run on either platform (or both). It is something that has been 
talked about for decades and never satisfactorily implemented, until now.

I get a bit tired of people slagging off MS when they don't really 
understand or use their most significant technical products. I really don't 
care about their applications like Office; if you prefer Open Office, go for 
it. I use MS Office 2003 and will probably move to Office 2007 at some 
point. It is easy to go with the flow, when it comes to applications. When 
it comes to technical development, it is a different story.

MicroSoft provides a bunch of languages that integrate to .NET, an IDE that 
is second to none (Visual Studio), has encouraged MILLIONS of young people 
to get into programming and provided free support, tutorials, and  forums 
for them, and they have made it all available for FREE.

Did your COBOL vendor ever do that? Even IBM at the height of their power 
never gave anyting away.

Yet, is "only because they want to market .NET".  Wanna know a secret? .NET 
would have gained acceptance whether they provided languages and an IDE for 
it, for free, or not. Having a language independent framework is just too 
important for developers to not use it. Developers would pay for the 
products (and do, to get full and comprehensive functionality). 
Nevertheless, I don't deny that giving people the chance to program for it, 
and making it really easy to do so, has helped promote it. So they made a 
good business decision, but we all benefit from it.

It doesn't matter what they do, in some quarters they will always be wrong.

If they join or support a standards body it is because they are "seeking to 
manipulate it". Nobody ever seems to give them any credit for having any 
integrity. Rather than being thankful, or even acknowledging that they have 
done much to bring computer programming to the masses, we get people just 
bashing them because they are MicroSoft.

Bottom line: .NET and C# are the RIGHT choice for Lee's Migration target. At 
least for the foreseeable future.

>  And I DO
> believe there have been problems reported with at least some of their
> products.  I don't need to spell out all the disadvantages of a monopoly.

You do for me...  Have you considered the advantages of a monopoly?  Sure we 
all like to have a choice, but a monopoly doesn't necessarily preclude that. 
Rather than just wave the banner of "Everybody knows..." why not sit down 
and look at some of the benefits that can be provided by a monopoly?  I was 
taught that a benevolent dictatorship is the most efficient form of 
government. I still don't want to live in one. But I won't argue against it 
on the grounds that "Everybody knows dictatorships are bad."

It is the final resort when arguing against MS... "Well, it's a 
monopoly...we don't need to spell out how bad that is..."

>
> Nothing lasts forever.  C# and .NET will someday be superceded.  Not that
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> that doesn't exist yet?

And WHAT IF people had bought Betamax instead of VHS?  Ever wondered why 
they didn't? We don't always get the products and services that we want. We 
invariably get the ones we deserve. (It's a bit like Governments in a 
Democracy, really...)

>
> Would it be possible to make it a two-step conversion: first step creates 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> second step would convert that to C#/.NET etc.

That's not a bad suggestion. It is rooted in the Waterfall and specifying 
stuff before you build it. In this case you have options when it comes to 
build. But it is a lot of work, and it is a rigid process that is not 
responsive to change. Development will be at least three times as long as if 
you DON'T do this.

What would the alternative be? Build for .NET and C# and simply evolve the 
tools by iteration and interaction with users. Use components and you can 
rearrange them at the drop of a hat. Flexible and fast.

You no longer have alternative build options and are committed to a single 
platform. But what exactly did having that option buy you? Just a choice. It 
slowed down your development and it bogged you into changing specs every 
time you amend something and all the concomitant regression testing and such 
that goes with it. Just so you have an option that you MIGHT exercise in the 
future. Not for me. I'll commit to .NET and if it gets superseded, I'll 
cross that bridge when I come to it.



>If it's possible, as you
> seem to have assumed or proved, that every facility in every language
> environment on every systyem can be converted to C# etc.,   then it's
> possible to create a set of specs that could be used in any language
> environment to create the necessary programs & forms, etc.

But why would you do that? We know the Waterfall simply doesn't work. (OK, 
I'll withdraw "we" and substitute "some of us"... :-))This comes down to an 
Agile, RAD, iterative, interactive, evolving approach versus a traditional 
Waterfall one. It's a no-contest.

>That way you
> aren't tied to a particular platform and are at least relatively immune to
> paradigm shifts.  Maintenance of the specification members, for 
> corrections
> & upgrades, would then be the same as for any other language.

And twice the work it needs to be. :-)

Pete.
"I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: My Migrations

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2008-10-01T09:40:06-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gv57e4l7cqm438kkm1djfu0s0je2r5gqrs@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad> <6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
We're moving to a PeopleSoft system.   We will have lots of tools,
objects, and even some languages which are proprietary.   This means,
that if we leave that environment, we will start from scratch.   But
I've found that the portability of CoBOL has been overstated.   Going
to a new system (in my experience) has always meant changing data,
processes, and business.

Portability matters in some industries that share processes.   I would
make real sure to test external web pages with every browser
available.    But for internal use - don't assume that existing code
will survive a system replacement.  
```

```
Interesting.   Why were they moved?   Were they so small that Unix

>>Portability matters in some industries that share processes.   I would
>>make real sure to test external web pages with every browser
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

And my experience has been with getting bigger and faster hardware
boxes almost transparently.    It isn't just CoBOL which stayed the
same then, but most everything else did as well.   (The biggest
challenge here was switching from IBM DOS to IBM OS in the shop I
worked in 1979).

The real conversions were to add functionality, which meant new
databases, new business solutions, etc.   They always came with new
hardware/OS to handle the new system.   We didn't convert old CoBOL
code but re-wrote the applications to the new needs.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: My Migrations

- **From:** billg999@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon)

```
In article <emf7e49e2a03u8rkruker7qvujdpdon6i3@4ax.com>,
	Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> writes:
> On Wed, 01 Oct 2008 12:35:05 -0500, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:
> 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> boxes ended up being cheaper?

Unix boxes often end up being cheaper and choosing Unix does not mean
something was small.  I watched one very large system (basicly, the
entire operation of a College) move from Honeywell to Univac and after
I had moved on it later migrated to a Unix System.  It never got smaller.
The equipment needed to run it did, however.  And, yes, it was all COBOL.
Unix does not neccessarily imply today's PC hardware.  There were and still
are some rather large machines running Unix.

bill
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: My Migrations

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-10-01T20:46:29-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<u638e4ti8l7hspoi7d17v2qaoc62mgqt4a@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad> <6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net> <gv57e4l7cqm438kkm1djfu0s0je2r5gqrs@4ax.com> <csc7e411l4o9lmd7oiodu8jrso2o9ghtar@4ax.com> <emf7e49e2a03u8rkruker7qvujdpdon6i3@4ax.com>`

```
On Wed, 01 Oct 2008 12:23:17 -0600, Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On Wed, 01 Oct 2008 12:35:05 -0500, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>boxes ended up being cheaper?

They moved because Unix boxes are cheaper than comparable mainframes, primarily because of
IBM's software licensing fees. 

Unix boxes can be bigger than mainframes.  HP SuperDomes have 64 or 128 CPUs, 1 TB of
memory, can process 15K transactions per second through Cobol programs. They cost well
over a million dollars. 

Of the world's 500 largest supercomputers, 495 run Unix, 5 run Windows, none run z/OS.

http://www.top500.org/stats/list/31/osfam

>>>Portability matters in some industries that share processes.   I would
>>>make real sure to test external web pages with every browser
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>code but re-wrote the applications to the new needs.

Usually, old needs did not disappear nor change. Applications are rewritten because the
DESIGN of the old code made adding new functionality difficult and eror-prone. That's
often caused by hidden coupling. In the old days, they did coupling with procedural flow
and called it spaghetti; now they do it with data.

Global structures are the least elegant form of data coupling. In Cobol, EVERY structure
in working-storage is global. Databases lend an air of legitimacy and the additional
pitfall  of coupling across time as well as space. 

The n-tier client/server model was a crude attempt to break the coupling habit, by making
lumps of code communicate through messages rather than data structures.

The coupling habit cannot be broken with technology, only with proper design.
```

```
Of course this has made Java improve too.

This is why it should _NOT_ be all one proprietary product, there

'Pressure groups' ?  MS has a business model that relies on
continually increasing revenue to keep the share price up. This allows
it to dilute the stock and pay its employees partly in share options.
This means that it saves cash (printing shares is 'free') and yet can
reduce its tax bill by counting the shares as payments.

It was easy when the PC market share was increasing but when the
market became saturated MS needed new markets: Servers, PDAs, Game
consoles, MP3 players, ...

MS wanted a share of the entertainment business, charging for music
and movie distribution. They needed to assure the movie industry that
they could control distribution and have them _only_ distribute via
MS.

What they forgot about, again, was the users. Most users do not want
DRM, when the company goes down the user loses their rights. Users
just want something that works like a CD does - without DRM crap.

I suppose 'pressuse groups' may be such as Dell and Gateway, ie MS's
customers. They wanted a new OS that required hardware upgrades.
Something that only needed the same hardware as XP, but was more
efficient would be useless to MS's customers (OEMs). Preferably it
would be 64 bit only so they _had_ to buy new everything.

Actually MS did announce that the next one after Vista would be 64bit
only so that people would buy more expensive machines this time
around.

The _only_ reason that Linux is an option to you is the GPL. If they
could have bought out the copyright holder(s) they would have and then
buried it. If Linux relied on OEMs they could have buried them as they
did with DRI, BeOS and many others.

The next revenue area for MS is the PC hardware itself. Or more
particularly the XPC. It is likely that a box based on XBox with
locked down BIOS so it will only run Windows is the next growth area
for MS. Instead of merely taking 15% - 20% of Dell/Gateway revenue
they can take 100% by building MS branded PCs and reserving the best
features for themselves while undercutting the prices until the OEMs
collapse.

In other words taking a leaf from Apple.

technical analyses and basically raved about Toyota. As a respected
publication it established the market for that brand in the US.

Toyota had 'bought the issue'. They paid the editors, the publishers
the printing costs, the distribution, they sold the advertising and
collected the revenue. It may have had Road & Track on the cover but
it was, as admitted later by R&T, a Toyota publication.

Microsoft does that too.

too.

Apple they need to keep around to 'prove' they are not a monopoly in

The same problem existed with HD and BlueRay. It was about control and
whether the media and the machines were available from multiple
vendors. At the moment it seems that DVD has won the battle and both
HD and Blueray will fail.
```

```
 > C# can run on either platform (or both). It is something that has
been
> talked about for decades and never satisfactorily implemented, until now.

Actually Java has been doing that for years. It even runs on my Nokia
hand held. Acu Cobol has been doing it for decades, MF got close. UCSD
did it in the 70s.

I have taken Python/Glade applications and put them on Linux, Windows,
my Nokia, ASUS eee, with zero changes.

[Pete]

Of course it will require you and your clients to buy each latest
version of Windows and use Windows servers.

No. It will require us to download the latest framework (for FREE) IF there

else's OS.

C#/.NET would have a good market share if it cost money, but MS wants
100% market share, it could only get that by giving it away, and by
paying Novell to pretend it would be cross-platform.
```

```
Wikpedia - Monopoly :
----------------------------------------------------------
DEFINITION :

In Economics, a monopoly (from Greek monos , alone or single + polein ,
to sell) exists when a specific individual or enterprise has sufficient
control over a particular product or service to determine significantly
the terms on which other individuals shall have access to it.[1]
Monopolies are thus characterized by a lack of economic competition for
the good or service that they provide and a lack of viable substitute
goods.[2] The verb "monopolize" refers to the process by which a firm
gains persistently greater market share than what is expected under
perfect competition.

A monopoly should be distinguished from monopsony, in which there is
only one buyer of a product or service; a monopoly may also have
monopsony control of a sector of a market. Likewise, a monopoly should
be distinguished from a cartel (a form of oligopoly), in which several
providers act together to coordinate services, prices or sale of goods.

A government-granted monopoly or legal monopoly is sanctioned by the
state, often to provide an incentive to invest in a risky venture or
enrich a domestic constituency. The government may also reserve the
venture for itself, thus forming a government monopoly.

POSITIVE ASPECTS :

Some argue that it can be good to allow a firm to attempt to monopolize
a market, since practices such as dumping can benefit consumers in the
short term; and once the firm grows too big, it can be dealt with via
regulation. When monopolies are not broken through the open market,
often a government will step in, either to regulate the monopoly, turn
it into a publicly owned monopoly environment, or forcibly break it up
(see Antitrust law). Public utilities, often being natural filiations
and less susceptible to efficient breakup, are often strongly regulated
or publicly owned. AT&T and Standard Oil are debatable examples of the
breakup of a private monopoly. When AT&T was broken up into the "Baby
Bell" components, MCI, Sprint, and other companies were able to compete
effectively in the long distance phone market and began to take phone
traffic from the less efficient AT&T server.

Examples of alleged and legal monopolies :-

     * The salt commission, a legal monopoly in China formed in 758.
     * British East India Company; created as a legal trading monopoly in
	 1600.

** we know from Sri Lankan friends that BEIC definitely affected the
Ceylon economy. Sri Lanka was self sufficient in rice production. The
British changed over to tea and I think, still today, Sri Lanka imports
rice.

     * Dutch East India Company; created as a legal trading monopoly in
	1602.
     * U.S. Steel; anti-trust prosecution failed in 1911.
     * Standard Oil; broken up in 1911.
     * National Football League; survived anti-trust lawsuit in the
  	1960s, convicted of being an illegal monopoly in the 1980s.
     * Major League Baseball; survived U.S. anti-trust litigation in
	1922, though its special status is still in dispute as of 2008.
     * United Aircraft and Transport Corporation; aircraft manufacturer
	holding company forced to divest itself of airlines in 1934.
     * American Telephone & Telegraph; telecommunications giant broken up
	 in 1982.
     * Microsoft; settled anti-trust litigation in the U.S. in 2001;
	fined by the European Commission in 2004, which was upheld for
	 the most part by the Court of First Instance of the European
	Communities in 2007. The fine was 1.35 Billion USD in 2008 for
	incompliance with the 2004 rule.[9][10]
     * De Beers; settled charges of price fixing in the diamond trade in
	the 2000s.
     * Joint Commission; has a monopoly over whether or not US hospitals
	are able to participate in the Medicare and Medicaid programs.
     * Telecom New Zealand; local loop unbundling enforced by central
	government.

**  How about that -  Kiwiland gets a mention :-)

     * Monsanto has been sued by competitors for anti-trust and
	monopolistic practices. They hold between 70% and 100% of the
	commercial seed market.

-----------------------------------------

Microsoft of course, (but Richard covered that EU-wise) but can't recall 
if he made reference to the various States' attorneys, and I was going 
to throw in WalMart for good measure.
```

```
Bill Gates has large share holdings in drug companies. The Melissa

purchasing policy, as in "we will give you X million drugs (high
retail, trivial production cost) if your purchasing policy eliminates
cheap copies of our expensive drugs.

The foundation then claims tax writeoffs at the highest level while
the shares rise in value.

They are not just giving it away.

Spyglass would be paid $5.00 for every copy _sold_. MS then gave it
away for free and didn't pay anything to Spyglass. When it was
included with Win98 it was obviously 'sold' as part of Win98 except
that the box stated 'includes a free copy of IE'.

You can read the actual history of this in the court records.
```

```
> [Pete] And you have shown that most of what you believe is speculative,
> based on your personal bias and not on facts.

Actually they are facts. You can check court records to see that MS
was found guilty of illegal 'per box pricing', that they settled
against the successors of DR-DOS for anti-trust violations, and many
others. Including they never paid Spyglass for writing IE for them.

> Persoanlly, I odn't really
> care how they got to where they are. I'm just glad they are there. If they
> weren't I'd still be paying a fortune to cobble away in COBOL and take all
> the aggravation and poor product support, with an archaic IDE that makes
> development like wading through molasses.

You may have stuck it out with that crap well beyond better stuff
being available, but just because you stepped from the 70s into .NET
doesn't mean that there was nothing in between.

You may have found that XP SP3 is the first OS that you are happy
with, but some of us found other stuff decades ago and find that XP
and .NET are just another thing in the mix and don't change the world
(not for us, maybe it did for you).


> [Pete] I don't read histories about companies; I talk and listen to people
> and I form my own conclusions.

You don't know the history yet you say my reporting of it is just
unfounded bias ??

> Nevertheless, I enjoy C# and for me it is "satisfactorily" cross platform.

Have you tried running your stuff on Mono ?

For one thing it is not quite .NET 2 yet, another is that it is GTK+
based and not WinForms. It omits many things that are Windows only.
It is possible to write a cross platform application but only by using
the common subset of stuff.

>
> [Pete]
> So I see MS is not your only prejudice... :-)

Quite right. I am also biased against American Imperialism.


> > If they join or support a standards body it is because they are "seeking
> > to
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pity someone didn't do that for COBOL...

Did you want the Cobol standard to be set by someone with the most
money regardless of the quality. Read the commentary about OOXML
(which MS have not implemented and probably never will).


> [Pete]
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> compilers and IDEs.

MS give away free Windows and software to schools and then claim the
retail price as a donation against tax. Given the cost is negligible
then they actually make money from this.  It is of course tied to
obligations from the schools, such as they will never run Linux.

'Free', I think not.

> Perhaps you could comment on the charitable contributions made by the Open
> Source Foundation? Ok, that's a cheap shot; we all know how tight their
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> wherewithal to make this kind of contribution. MS do it,



> Bill Gates has
> personally given away billions of dollars, and you say they have no
> integrity. They don't HAVE to do this.

Bill Gates has large share holdings in drug companies. The Melissa
Gates foundation gives away millions of, say, AIDS drugs, made by the
company he has shares in. This donation is tied to the countries drug
purchasing policy, as in "we will give you X million drugs (high
retail, trivial production cost) if your purchasing policy eliminates
cheap copies of our expensive drugs.

The foundation then claims tax writeoffs at the highest level while
the shares rise in value.

They are not just giving it away.


> [Pete]
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> now.

I was taking notice in the 80s and 90s. You obviously were not.

> MS today is a different company than it was when they first produced IE.

IE was written by Spyglass based on Mosaic. The contract stated that
Spyglass would be paid $5.00 for every copy _sold_. MS then gave it
away for free and didn't pay anything to Spyglass. When it was
included with Win98 it was obviously 'sold' as part of Win98 except
that the box stated 'includes a free copy of IE'.

You can read the actual history of this in the court records.

> [Pete]
>
> <snipped remaining fanciful rant>

> [Pete]
>
> Like I said, I wouldn't know...

I think that sums up your attitude nicely.

You know little and think that this is 'made up'.

[Pete]

No, Richard, I don't think you made it up and I do think some of the points 
you made are fair.

Again, it was a very good response.

But we are coming from fundamentally different viewpoints.

Your is anti-American, anti MicroSoft (which you have a perfect right to 
hold and I can even agree with some of your objections), mine is not. I see 
a company providing products to me which I find incredibly useful and they 
don't cost me a penny. (Well, they do eventually because I get all fired up 
and want the full version, but I do consider them value for money.:-))

You are correct when you say that I may have missed other products that 
could do the same. I was engrossed with COBOL even though I knew it couldn't 
last. So you are also correct when you say that for me the leap from COBOL 
to .NET was kind of like an epiphany. I accept that you had already done 
similar without using MS products.

As for the history of MS, of course I know something about it but I'm not as 
interested as you are. What I am interested in is what a product can do for 
me.

You asked if I have tried Mono. The answer is "yes". Some time ago, just as 
an exercise. I found the limitations you mentioned and, for me, the fact 
that it had no InterOP services means that I couldn't use it in any 
meaningful way. (A major activity for me with .NET is leveraging COBOL 
Legacy, as demonstrated in the recent exercise.) However, I did get a basic 
C# program to work in both Mono and .NET, so the concept is sound.

OK, I think you have made some excellent points, I understand your 
viewpoint, but disagree with it fundamentally.

Are we done now? :-)

Pete.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad> <6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net>`

```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net...
>
>
<snip>

Thanks to Richard for his comprehensive post answering PD's points.  While
Richard also disagrees with me on why MS has prevailed, it's clear that he
is in "alignment" with me in attributing it to causes that do not
include"tecnhological superiority".


> >  And I DO
> > believe there have been problems reported with at least some of their
> > products.  I don't need to spell out all the disadvantages of a
monopoly.
>
> You do for me...  Have you considered the advantages of a monopoly?  Sure
we
> all like to have a choice, but a monopoly doesn't necessarily preclude
that.
> Rather than just wave the banner of "Everybody knows..." why not sit down
> and look at some of the benefits that can be provided by a monopoly?  I
was
> taught that a benevolent dictatorship is the most efficient form of
> government. I still don't want to live in one. But I won't argue against
it
> on the grounds that "Everybody knows dictatorships are bad."
>

I did not say or imply "everybody knows".  The source of my opinion is the
mass of documentation that's been published in the popular and technical
press.  My personal experiences with MS products have not been all that bad,
but there are innumerable smaller things that irk or don't seem to work the
way they should - or go in a direction that seems unfathomable but which I
must put up with; as I have worked with competing products, such as Open
Office, I do know where things need to be improved or when somebody else has
done them better.  Nonetheless, my experience is not sufficiently broad to
go further than the irony I spake above.

Why are monopolies bad?  Because they abuse their powers and stifle
innovation.  Businesses exist to make money - no other reason.  They will do
anything to that end, legal or otherwise; if legal, fine; if not, they'll
push against the limits and slowly extend them and continuously redefine the
limits to include "we can get away with this because it's insignificant" and
"nobody will fight us on this because we've been getting away with it so
long".

Abuses of power by monopolies (or oligolopolies/cartels) are too common to
list.  As examples: the oil industry, the banking and insurance industries.
Depending on where you live, the cost of gasoline is overpriced by a factor
of 2 to 10 (taxes not withstanding).  Et cetera.  And as Richard points out,
"everybody knows" MS has been found guilty of this.

More specifically to the IT industry: you've been around for a while: you
may remember these little incidents.  In the Olde days when IBM owned the
world, at least once they increased their prices to improve their profit
margin: they actually came out and said it!  Astoundingly, nearly all the
commentators thought it was a good thing.  When the 4300 series was
announced, they invited I/T managers from every computer site to
introductory seminars; the kicker was that they wanted you to sign a letter
of intent to get one before you left.  Well, I didn't, but I don't know
about some of the others.  Anyway, there were so many orders that IBM
decided to make a bit of dough off that fact alone: instead of filling them
first-come, first served as would be proper, they set up a lottery to
determine the shipping sequence!  A secondary market developed where the
assigned sequence became tradeable: IBM charged a small fee on each such
trade.  IBM was found guilty of monopolistic practices, but the appeals
dragged on, and with a change of administration in the US the charges were
dropped, by order!

It was exceedingly frustrating if you worked for one of the BUNCH at that
time; didn't matter that you could point out your bid was better in every
respect than the Hungry I's bid, it didn't matter: you'd hear "We know we
have the best so we needn't look anywhere else"  I was at Univac: we lost
one order because IBM had the right colour scheme!  That only cost them
about $20k a month.  I did a study of the IBM proposals I could find; all of
them cautioned that the configuration listed was not necessarily the one
that would be sold to the customer; nor would they guarantee that the
machine they delivered would actually do the job: none of their customers
objected, or, it seemed, even noticed.  Now all this came about because of
marketing and the bandwagon; IBM made a point of getting to the young and
eager people that came into the field (I was one of them to be sure, and I
got in lots of trouble at Univac until I stopped talking about the IBM 1130)
who, once indoctrinated, would like all young people be firm in the faith
forever.  Young people become older people who order computers - by that
time they didn't even get quotes.  When the 370/155 was announced, a friend
proudly told me that he'd called in their order at 8:31on the day of the
announcement.

That's what MS has done by giving away its products.  Anybody that can do
this will, because it works so well but I have ethical problems with the
practice; in MS's case it has definitely been used to stifle competition.

You may well laugh when I suggest that monopolies stifle innovation.
Richard makes a very good case, but you'll undoubtedly point out the
enormous amount of work and new applications that have been done using.NET
etc.  There was a ton of worthy work done when the PC's first came out,
using the languages that were available then; but nobody had a monopoly then
so other methods were developed.  Now that MS rules the world, there are far
fewer people to come up with different methods - quite often, something new
is an alternative that does the same thing a different way, or bolts on to
what is there.  Genuinely different approaches are rare and unlikely, and
certainly face a vertical uphill battle - because with MS's dominance few
will listen, fewer will adopt.  And as Richard points out, MS will buy them
out anyway.

What monopolies do is subtle, if they are clever:  innovation appears to be
flourishing, but it's within certain limits, which may not be readily
perceptible.  Certainly, if you're devoted heart & sould to an environment
you will not easily become aware of restraints or problems - or, you may
become aware of them but faithfully accept them as inevitable.  I don't know
if MS is capable of subtlety but lacking an alternative with sufficient
resources to point out the differences they don't need to be.

I have a few for-instances.

Consider the Intel instruction set.  Who's to say whether the IBM (BAL)
instruction set wouldn't work as well or better on PC's?

Quite a long time ago, in the early days of the Internet, I read a number of
articles on an alternative method of widespread communications: to treat all
the machines on the network as being part of one gigantic memory space
rather than as independent physical entities.  Results were said to be
promising.  But browsers were already in control of that market.

More recently, there was a series by someone repected questioning the whole
von Neumann concept as it's been implemented.  I hope I can find them again
because it looked very promising.

Anyway, the point is that we'll never know about any of these other than as
academic speculation.  The de facto monopolies in all facets of computer
systems will ensure that such concepts never break out.  Unless goverment
supported!


> It is the final resort when arguing against MS... "Well, it's a
> monopoly...we don't need to spell out how bad that is..."
>

Fiddle.


>
> >
> > Would it be possible to make it a two-step conversion: first step
creates
> > an
> > agnostic, platform-independent, language-independent "specification"
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> responsive to change. Development will be at least three times as long as
if
> you DON'T do this

We aren't talking about development.  We are talking about conversion.

Lee's process, I'm certain, must be a two-pass thing.  From my own
experiments in trying to develop a universal generator, I know that you
can't blat out the target language statement immediately for each input
statement: even for as simple a statment as A = B + C there are several task
to be accomplished.    A Fortran FORMAT, an RPG SETON, a Cobol EVALUATE -
not to mention all the 3GL's he hints at - there are lots of setups which
need to be done and which must wait until all the source statements are in.
So Lee must have to develop tables and subfiles and lists which are THEN
manipulated to create the target code.  What I'm asking is if that
intermediate collection can't be formalized and saved?  Then it could be
used to develop on ANY target platform.  Your and Lee's preferences aside,
there is value in alternative outputs.

>
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> I'll withdraw "we" and substitute "some of us"... :-))This comes down to
an
> Agile, RAD, iterative, interactive, evolving approach versus a traditional
> Waterfall one. It's a no-contest.

Conversion, not development!!!  And Waterfall methods do work.  They are for
all purposes obsolete, but they work.

>
> Pete.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

You make a mighty reply to a small post.  Methinks thou dost protest too
much.

Cheers
PL
```

```
"Bullshit baffles brains"). Not aimed at your references specifically at

Fannie and Freddie became monopolies by a lack of ethics in how they 
sold mortgages. Their greed initiated the current U.S. financial 
meltdown - but in modern commerce with globalised corporations that's a 
GLOBAL meltdown

If I remember correctly one of Bill K's styles is "Bottom Line....". So 
here goes :-

Bottom Line Ronnie Regan's, "Less government ......" has come home to 
roost. Kept to an ABSOLUTE minimum, governments must have overall rules 
that cover how corporations may function financially, internally or 
globally.

Further, our accounting brethren are to blame; if the bean-counting 
profession wont do it, then governments MUST :-

(1)  The format of Balance Sheets and P & Ls must be changed so that a 
reasonably educated person can interpret what they are saying.

(2) There must be rules as to how assets are evaluated - so often,
( over the past ten years ?), when we see corporations folding, there 
are quite often asset value problems. (Just in case somebody doesn't get 
the point - example. A chunk of Athabasca Tarsands is shown on the books 
valued at say $ 1.5 billion based on the geologist's estimate of how 
many barrels of crude that will produce, over the projected life cycle. 
For whatever reason, subsequent evaluation by the geologist reduces the 
estimate by 25% - so now how does that asset appear in the books).

I'm envious. I wish I could express myself in the conciseness that you do.
```

```
On Oct 3, 8:23 am, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
> Pete Dashwood <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message


> IBM was found guilty of monopolistic practices, but the appeals
> dragged on, and with a change of administration in the US the charges were
> dropped, by order!

Not quite. They were split into several divisions and were banned from
pre-announcing products. Basically as soon as a competitor announced
they had a product IBM would do a me-too announcement just to get the
customers to wait to see rather than buy the one that was available.

IBM were restricted by being required to ship within 3 months of
announcement. This was used by MS over an OS/2 release. IBM were
entitled to use any _released_ version of Windows. They had pre-
releases of Win3.11 which they incorporated into OS/2 WARP. The plan
was to ship WARP as soon as Win3.11 shipped.  MS simply held up 3.11
shipping until the 3 months were up and IBM _had_ to ship. They had to
go back to only Win3.1 for WARP, then 3.11 was shipped.

MS pre-announce vapourware all the time. I had related already how
they saw the GEM demo then announced 'Windows' and took 2 years to
write it.

Google has recently made some news with 'Cloud Computing'. MS has
announced 'Windows Cloud' just so that people will stop and wait to
see what the MS product is like. Actually they probably don't have
anything yet but will put something together over the next few months,
as long as people will still hang onto their tails instead of buying
into a non-MS product.

Windows 7 was announced just so that those that didn't want Vista
would wait around for another year or so in the hope that MS may
actually develop something better.

> that would be sold to the customer; nor would they guarantee that the
> machine they delivered would actually do the job: none of their customers
> objected, or, it seemed, even noticed.

I recall one proposal where IBM stated that their machine would run
the accounting system and could run the production system and could do
some other stuff. It was sized too small and was cheaper. The customer
bought it and found that a machine twice the size was required. IBM
answered that its proposal stated it could do any [one] of these
things. The customer was mistaken in thinking it said it do them all
at once.

> What monopolies do is subtle, if they are clever:  innovation appears to be
> flourishing, but it's within certain limits, which may not be readily
> perceptible.

MS claim innovation all the time, yet they are usually a couple of
years behind what the leading edge is actually doing.

ignored it. Later Win95 tried to avoid the internet by offering MSN
(the original) which was Win95 only and did not allow access to the
internet. They had to dump this and Win95 OSR2 had IE. Nowadays the
general public think that MS invented the internet and Bill Gates
wrote it himself.
```

```
was to ship WARP as soon as Win3.11 shipped.  MS simply held up 3.11
shipping until the 3 months were up and IBM _had_ to ship. They had to
go back to only Win3.1 for WARP, then 3.11 was shipped.

write it.

announced 'Windows Cloud' just so that people will stop and wait to
see what the MS product is like. Actually they probably don't have
anything yet but will put something together over the next few months,
as long as people will still hang onto their tails instead of buying
into a non-MS product.

Windows 7 was announced just so that those that didn't want Vista
would wait around for another year or so in the hope that MS may
actually develop something better.

at once.
```

```
I enjoyed reading it, Peter. It is a good response.

I did expound a bit, but it is because I do find certain MS products 
incredibly useful and it is annoying to see people who have never used them, 
don't really understand the purpose of them, decry them just because they 
are from MicroSoft, or because they don't know how to remove the little 
doggie from a search screen, or configure MS Word so it does what they want, 
and then hold MS responsible for their own ignorance and unwillingness to 
learn.

However, on reflection, I realise I shouldn't let this get to me, and I 
won't in future.

Your points on monopolies are not worth arguing. I don't disagree strongly 
enough to argue it. My dissension was not about whether monopolies are good 
or not, it was about arguing it on the basis of "everybody knows"...

I enjoyed very much your trip down memory lane with IBM in the old days. I, 
too lived through that era. I saw things every bit as bad as what you 
described, even downright wicked.

(Like getting EDP managers removed because they wouldn't toe the party 
line... or even having the competence of a highly competent manager brought 
into question because he didn't buy a certain product...."What kind of fool 
do you have running your computer department. We just showed him a product 
that is exactly what you need and he can't see it." This at Director level 
over a few beers... Wicked stuff. Makes MS look like amateurs... It was 
seeing stuff like this that encouraged me to work in environments other than 
IBM and I ended up working on all of the BUNCH apart from Univac...although 
I did work on Burroughs, which later merged with Univac to become Unisys. 
(You may remember... "The power of two"  ...:-)))

Running the IBM architecture on a PC...

With today's technology it would certainly be possible. When Gene Amdahl 
designed system 360 his concept of a flexible machine that could be easily 
reconfigured (implemented through mylar ROS cards) was quite revolutionary. 
The battle between RISC and CISC was still not settled and the 360 CISC had 
some great advantages from a marketing point of view. (You could slow it 
down with ROS cards and so provide a marketable range, with a single 
processor, that could be upgraded by removing or exchanging the card that 
was slowing it down...). Other systems that were more RISC like creamed it 
in the benchmarks because it spent a lot of time stepping through ROS to 
find out how to execute its complex instructions.  (IBM marketed it by 
saying that it could do in one instruction what other machines would take 
four to do, but they didn't mention it took 5 times as long :-)) 
Nevertheless, it was a big success for IBM.Things like the decimal 
instruction set (packed), which was optional and implemented in ROS on early 
360s, later got moved to true hardware to improve the performance of it.)

I think you could definitely emulate the architecture on a modern PC, but 
why would you want to? Are you suggesting that the BAL architecure is 
superior to the Intel or Motorola architectures? I can assure you, it isn't. 
It was brilliant for its time, but that time is long gone. It exists today 
because it is buried in legacy, not because it is the best architecture.

OK, enough from me. Gotta do some work... :-)

I enjoyed your response. Thanks.

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad> <6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net> <af9Fk.13241$ex3.10736@newsfe02.iad> <6kl125F8bkpgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:6kl125F8bkpgU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> Running the IBM architecture on a PC...

> I think you could definitely emulate the architecture on a modern PC, but
> why would you want to? Are you suggesting that the BAL architecure is
> superior to the Intel or Motorola architectures? I can assure you, it
isn't.
> It was brilliant for its time, but that time is long gone. It exists today
> because it is buried in legacy, not because it is the best architecture.
>


A further thought: if IBM had actually used the BAL instruction set for
their PC, or a subset of it, I'd wager that we would never have heard of
"expanded memory", "extended memory", "upper and lower memory blocks", the
unaddressable top 64K of memory - all the things that could be inserted into
the dictionary replacing the word "kludge".  Also, we'd have had
multiprogramming a lot sooner, and would have heard less of memory leaks.

(This isn't due to any intrinsic superiority of the BAL set.  It's just that
all the O/S progamming experience that had been done with it would certainly
have been drawn upon).

It didn't occur to me to ask at the time: what was the reasoning behind
adopting the "foreign" instruction set and architecture?  Becuase it was
available and they were in a hurry?

PL
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7f97c7c8-e740-44be-a964-534cde7c0e43@79g2000hsk.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad> <6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net> <af9Fk.13241$ex3.10736@newsfe02.iad> <6kl125F8bkpgU1@mid.individual.net> <P%qFk.61531$hX5.27354@newsfe06.iad>`

```
On Oct 4, 4:37 am, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
> Pete Dashwood <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> their PC, or a subset of it,

It would have cost several thousand just for the CPU chip.

Actually IBM had used microprocessors before. The System 23 used a
Z80. The 5100 series were very simple cpus with microcode.

>  I'd wager that we would never have heard of
> "expanded memory", "extended memory", "upper and lower memory blocks", the
> unaddressable top 64K of memory - all the things that could be inserted into
> the dictionary replacing the word "kludge".

Many micros in the late 70s used bank-switching so they could use more
than 64Kb of memory. The IBM PC didn't invent these things they
adopted them from what had gone before.

Upper and lower memory was on mainframes.

> Also, we'd have had
> multiprogramming a lot sooner,

We did, for those that noticed. DRIs MP/M was released in 1978 and ran
on 8080/8085/Z80 machines with bank-switched memory. Oasys ran on the
same hardware (I have some Onyxs in my stack). MP/M-86 for 8088/8086
was in 1981. When IBM and MS were announcing IBM-PC XT and MS-DOS 2
which could finally do hard disks* DRI was demonstrating Concurrent-CP/
M-86 which (like MP/M) was pre-emptive multitasking with virtual
screens (ie a keystroke switched from one task to another switching
the screen buffer).

DRIs DOS-Plus which came standard with the Amstrad 1500 (along with MS-
DOS 2.11) and the BBC 512 was a cut down version of Concurrent but had
'background tasks'.

There were many other multitasking and multiuser systems on Z80 and
8086, some graphics based.

Microsoft was always a couple of years behind and did most things
quite poorly. When they made a multi-tasking MS-DOS it just wasn't any
good and was cancelled. MS-DOS 4 (based on 3.1 and not to be confused
with the much later MS-DOS 4.01), also known as European DOS, was
available on ICL, Siemanns and Wang PCs for a short time. There was
even a 4.1 based on 3.2 (ie before 3.3) but it just wasn't much use.
It reduced the memory available, most stuff didn't work well with it.
This is mainly because MS-DOS software tended to do direct screen
writes (because using DOS or BIOS was too slow) and sat in keyhit()
loops rather than doing a DOS keyboard read. This then used 100% CPU
while doing nothing.

MS-DOS programs also tended to use segemt manipulation on the 8088
which, of course, didn't work on the 80286. This crippled the PC ATs
as 286s were used just as faster 8086s, except for OS/2 and other
better systems.


> and would have heard less of memory leaks.

Memory leaks are not a function of expanded/extended memory.


> It didn't occur to me to ask at the time: what was the reasoning behind
> adopting the "foreign" instruction set and architecture?  Becuase it was
> available and they were in a hurry?

IBM had many different instruction sets. IBM System/3s were not 360s.
The IBM 5100s were completely different as were Series 1 and other
machines.

The IBM 5150 PC was thrown together from stuff they had laying around,
the planar came from the System 23 they had been working on for some
months. This was Zilog Z80 based and so the PC was to be that until
Intel convinced them the new 8088 would fit (it was 8bit bus) with
only minor changes to the circuits.

Soon afterwards the IBM PC 360 Emulator came out running 2 Motorola
68000 CPUs and another chip I forget.


* To have a hard disk on an IBM-PC (pre XT) just use DRIs CP/M-86 and
add a Xibec controller and a disk, it worked 'out of the box'.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: My Migrations: PC architecture

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<564a2d9d-ca91-4b33-8fbf-9525a2f9fd09@p31g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad> <6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net> <af9Fk.13241$ex3.10736@newsfe02.iad> <6kl125F8bkpgU1@mid.individual.net> <P%qFk.61531$hX5.27354@newsfe06.iad> <7f97c7c8-e740-44be-a964-534cde7c0e43@79g2000hsk.googlegroups.com> <6uxFk.41$cX6.27@newsfe07.iad>`

```
On Oct 4, 11:59 am, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
> Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> [/pl]

The attraction of the IBM PC was that it was 'only' $4,000 (for a
basic machine). The previous IBM 'personal' machines, the 5100 series,
which had IBM designed processors, were $9,000 to $20,000.


>
> Actually IBM had used microprocessors before. The System 23 used a
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> Bank switching isn't the same thing.

'Bank Switching' is exactly what EMS and EEMS is. In the case of EMS
it switches memory in the address space above 640Kb. The program
fiddles registers to switch one block out and another in.

"""Expanded memory - Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
Expanded memory was a system of bank switching introduced around 1984
that provided more memory to byte-hungry, business-oriented MS-DOS
programs. ..."""

With EEMS (eg AST RAMPage) the bank switching is anywhere above the
planar memory. With Concurrent-DOS on IBM PC XT and AT the planar
memory was stripped down to 256Kb and a RAMPage card with 2 or 4
megabyte would fill in the address space to 640 Kb which could be bank
switched on context change by resetting a few registers.

XMS was an emulation of bank switching that relied on protected mode
transitions to get the data from above the 1Mbyte limit into an
accessible area above 640Kb.

> And I never heard of UMB's etc. until
> they were used with MS DOS.
> [/pl]

Your not hearing of them was not a limitation on the inventiveness of
prior computers.

The terms 'upper memory' and 'lower memory' were used on the ICL 1900
series in the mid 60s and may well have been used on other machines.
'Lower memory' was the first 4Kb words of the program address space
and this could be directly referenced by the instruction. Above that
the upper memory required use of an index register to access the
memory.


> > Also, we'd have had
> > multiprogramming a lot sooner,
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> [/pl]

Memory leaks are caused by the program aquiring memory and then not
releasing it or reusing it, merely requesting more each time. The
problem is independent of where it is aquired from.

Most things in MS-DOS were merely copied from other systems and done
badly - and that continues with Windows.
```

```
On Fri, 3 Oct 2008 10:37:29 -0500, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>
>Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>the dictionary replacing the word "kludge".

IBM doesn't learn from its mistakes. The 360 was a 32 bit machine that, for no good
reason, used only 24 bits to address memory, thus putting a 16M limit on the size of
memory.

The Intel 808x was a 16 bit machine capable of a 28 bit memory address: 16 bit segment and
12 bit offset. It could have handled 256M of memory, but Intel decided, for no good
reason, to limit it to 1M. Then IBM reserved 360K (was the number a coincidence?) for
device drivers it called BIOS. Those device drivers were soon replaced by more capable
software ones, but the address space remained lost.

Both limits were mistakes that required extraordinary effort to overcome.

>  Also, we'd have had
>multiprogramming a lot sooner, and would have heard less of memory leaks.

>(This isn't due to any intrinsic superiority of the BAL set.  It's just that
>all the O/S progamming experience that had been done with it would certainly
>have been drawn upon).

OS/2 was obviously written by MVS guys, because its internal structures used the same
terminology as MVS internals. Its failure disproves the assertion that mainframe expertise
would have prevailed. 

>It didn't occur to me to ask at the time: what was the reasoning behind
>adopting the "foreign" instruction set and architecture?  Becuase it was
>available and they were in a hurry?

The reason is IBM wasn't capable of cranking out a million CPU chips for $300 per, nor
produce peripheral controllers in the volume required. 

The mainframe universe, then and now, was comprised of 10K boxes , each yielding 90% gross
margin. You might as well ask why MacDonald's doesn't use the same recipes as Charlie
Trotter or why Charlie doesn't have drivethru windows.  It's all food, isn't it?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: My Migrations: PC architecture

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>

- **References:** `<LIadnU0MEIKYQ3zVnZ2dnUVZ_ovinZ2d@comcast.com> <uhsEk.657$ex3.20@newsfe02.iad> <6kfrnhF7lvf0U1@mid.individual.net> <af9Fk.13241$ex3.10736@newsfe02.iad> <6kl125F8bkpgU1@mid.individual.net> <P%qFk.61531$hX5.27354@newsfe06.iad> <oh9de4lrfqg1lvtdj2bn4o4765s9vuc7tu@4ax.com>`

```
On Oct 4, 1:13 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Fri, 3 Oct 2008 10:37:29 -0500, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> memory.

You are wrong, there were many _very_good_ reasons (at least as good
as Doc's), such as 'no one will ever need more than ...' and saving
one quarter of the circuit boards in the address translator, and using
the bits for another task.

> The Intel 808x was a 16 bit machine capable of a 28 bit memory address: 16 bit segment and
> 12 bit offset. It could have handled 256M of memory, but Intel decided, for no good
> reason, to limit it to 1M.

It would have required millions (ok probably thousands) more
transistors in the chip to use a 28 bit address. It also would have
poor granularity with most of the memory address space being wasted.
They tried it with 286 protected mode but that was a complete failure
because it would run out of registers to hold segment addresses.

> Then IBM reserved 360K (was the number a coincidence?) for
> device drivers it called BIOS.

Actually the number was 384Kb. 1024Kb minus 640Kb is 384Kb.

> Those device drivers were soon replaced by more capable
> software ones, but the address space remained lost.

Not true at all for the 8088/8086 based machines. The IBM PC used the
address space for memory mapped screens as well as the BIOS and that
area was always addressable.

> Both limits were mistakes that required extraordinary effort to overcome.
>

No. What was a mistake was trying to use a toy machine that was mostly
obsolete when it was introduced (the IBM PC) and a toy operating
system that was a poorly implemented clone (or copy) of a six year old
system (CP/M) that had been built with the limitations of 64Kb in
mind.

The poor implementation of the BIOS and of MS-DOS meant that basic
operations, such as screen i-o were very slow and lacked
functionality. To get reasonable speed and facility (such as colour)
it was necessary to bit-bang the hardware (eg direct screen writes).

Programmers used segment arithmetic on pointers to access data faster
and this led to code that was incompatible with better processors and
prevented adoption of 286 protected mode and OS/2.

Intel had made a chip that was 'the best 8bit micro', IBM had made a
machine a few percent better than the Apple ][ (eg more memory, faster
CPU, 160Kb vs 120Kb disks) to stop intrusion into their space and as a
terminal - serial and 3740.

The mistake was by the programmers who, impressed by the brand name,
thought they could use these for real work instead of using properly
designed machines running useful operating systems and, since the mid
70s, doing it with networking, shared drives and multi-tasking.

> >  Also, we'd have had
> >multiprogramming a lot sooner, and would have heard less of memory leaks.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> would have prevailed.

Actually OS/2 was originally written by Microsoft. Granted it was to
IBMs spec. MS-DOS 4.0 (not to be confused with the later 4.01) was
designed by MS and was a failure. IBM speced MS-DOS 5 (not the be
confused with the later MS-DOS 5) to be a 286 based OS and it was
later named OS/2.  When Microsoft had stolen all the useful bits out
of OS/2's design and incorporated them into Windows-286 and Windows
extended mode (3.0), it spat the dummy and IBM had to rescue the code
it had paid MS to write.

> >It didn't occur to me to ask at the time: what was the reasoning behind
> >adopting the "foreign" instruction set and architecture?  Becuase it was
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> produce peripheral controllers in the volume required.

Wrong again. IBM intended that they would sell no more than 50,000 of
these machines based on their mainframe sites and the number of Apple ]
[s already there.  They built it as a follow on from the 5100 series
and as a better System 23. The IBM PC model number was 5150 following
on fro 5100, 51010, 5120.

They did not expect to make millions of them at all and they used
existing parts that they were _already_ using in other machines,
except the 8088 replaced the Z80 of the System 23.

> The mainframe universe, then and now, was comprised of 10K boxes , each yielding 90% gross
> margin. You might as well ask why MacDonald's doesn't use the same recipes as Charlie
> Trotter or why Charlie doesn't have drivethru windows.  It's all food, isn't it?
```

```
On Sun, 5 Oct 2008 12:07:16 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Oct 4, 1:13ï¿½pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Fri, 3 Oct 2008 10:37:29 -0500, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>the bits for another task.

They used one bit for problem/supervisor state and four bits for memory protection key,
thereby limiting the number of running programs to 15 (no one will ever need more). That
was in the Program Status Word (PSW), a single register. The upper eight bits were wasted
in other registers and pointers in memory.

Thirty years after the limit was removed (with XA), mainframers are STILL dealing with
above-the-line/below-the-line issues. 

>> The Intel 808x was a 16 bit machine capable of a 28 bit memory address: 16 bit segment and
>> 12 bit offset. It could have handled 256M of memory, but Intel decided, for no good
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>because it would run out of registers to hold segment addresses.

Fragmentation is not important on a virtual memory machine like the 286. Unused portions
of the 1TB virtual address space (per process) were not mapped to real memory.

>> Those device drivers were soon replaced by more capable
>> software ones, but the address space remained lost.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>area was always addressable.

It used 64K for memory mapped screens. The other 320K was wasted. 128K, C000 and D000,
wasn't even used, it was reserved for unspecified add-ons, which were on their own to
resolve conflicts. 

There was no good reason for the screen or anything else to be at fixed locations.  The
BIOS could have allocated segments dynamically.

>> Both limits were mistakes that required extraordinary effort to overcome.
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>mind.

The design team, headed by Don Estridge, had one year to get the PC to market. As an Entry
Systems Division (ESD) outpost in Boca Raton Fl, they had little support from or influence
over mainstream IBM. They decided to go with components readily available from mostly
non-IBM sources. Think of them as a company started in a garage. 

After IBM saw how successful the PC was, it pulled the PC back into the corporate fold,
which produced flops including the PC Jr (make it into toy), 3270 PC (make it into a
terminal) and PS2  (make it proprietary).

>The poor implementation of the BIOS and of MS-DOS meant that basic
>operations, such as screen i-o were very slow and lacked
>functionality. To get reasonable speed and facility (such as colour)
>it was necessary to bit-bang the hardware (eg direct screen writes).

My device drivers offered a choice between direct, BIOS and DOS screen writes. 
BIOS wasn't objectionably slow; DOS was. The driver filtered out unnecessary writes of
characters that had not changed. 

Especially troublesome was displaying graphics on a monochrome adaptor by Hercules and
clones. Because card makers never produced a BIOS (I did), nearly all software support for
graphics was for CGA only. To show it on a mono screen, I had to change its aspect ratio.
It was a challenge to do that with animated games fast enough to avoid ghosting and
noticable lag. 

>Programmers used segment arithmetic on pointers to access data faster
>and this led to code that was incompatible with better processors and
>prevented adoption of 286 protected mode and OS/2.

It was never necessary to do arithmetic on segment addresses. I never did it, don't know
why anyone would.

>Intel had made a chip that was 'the best 8bit micro',

No, it was clearly a 16 bit CPU.

> IBM had made a
>machine a few percent better than the Apple ][ (eg more memory, faster
>CPU, 160Kb vs 120Kb disks) to stop intrusion into their space and as a
>terminal - serial and 3740.

I don't think IBM corporate had a clear vision of the PC's role. Some saw it as a toy,
some as competition for their low end 52xx single user machines, some as a smart terminal.
I saw it as a tiny mainframe. 

>The mistake was by the programmers who, impressed by the brand name,
>thought they could use these for real work instead of using properly
>designed machines running useful operating systems and, since the mid
>70s, doing it with networking, shared drives and multi-tasking.

IN 1984 I ran a drag race between a PC with 30M hard drive versus a 4361 (small
mainframe), running the same Cobol program doing 'typical' batch processing VSAM
operations. The PC won. 

I then proceeded to write a multi-tasking application under MSDOS, with cooperative task
switching in the application. Each PC ran four tasks; each task handled two modems -- one
data and one voice/DTMF. We ran ten PCs talking to up to 40 users.

>> > ï¿½Also, we'd have had
>> >multiprogramming a lot sooner, and would have heard less of memory leaks.
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>it had paid MS to write.

OS/2 is a good example of infighting between IBM divisions. IBM's own PC marketing
division was selling PCs bundled  with Windows. They, along with Microsoft, targeted home
users; OS/2 developers were trying to replace MVS; other IBMers were pushing Linux.
```
