```
On Oct 7, 4:25 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

> >> IBM doesn't learn from its mistakes. The 360 was a 32 bit machine that, for no good
> >> reason, used only 24 bits to address memory, thus putting a 16M limit on the size of
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> They used one bit for ...

It was irony.

> >> The Intel 808x was a 16 bit machine capable of a 28 bit memory address: 16 bit segment and
> >> 12 bit offset. It could have handled 256M of memory, but Intel decided, for no good
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> of the 1TB virtual address space (per process) were not mapped to real memory.

Your original was that "the 808x ... could have handled 256M of
memory". It would have wasted most of it because each segment would
use 64Kb regardless if it was smaller. Considering that the 5150 Model
A could only use 256Kb memory and that 16Kb cost $90.00 it was rather
useful to allow a 16 byte granularity.

The 80286 did not have a 1Tb address space because it only had 8000 or
so selectors.

> >> Those device drivers were soon replaced by more capable
> >> software ones, but the address space remained lost.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> resolve conflicts.

But the address space could be used, it wasn't lost. Certainly it did
need an add-on, such as an EMS card to fill in with usable RAM.

> There was no good reason for the screen or anything else to be at fixed locations.  The
> BIOS could have allocated segments dynamically.

Cost. That's a good reason. Anyway the Model A could only address
256Kb so why worry about what happens at address that are physically
impossible. It was competing against machines with 64Kb.

> >> Both limits were mistakes that required extraordinary effort to overcome.
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> non-IBM sources.

If you had read further down the Wiki you would have seen that their
aim was not very high, just catching up with machines that had been
out for 3 years or more already.

"""The original line of PCs were part of an IBM strategy to get into
the small computer market then dominated by the Commodore PET, Atari 8-
bit family, Apple II and Tandy Corporation's TRS-80s, and various CP/M
machines."""

> Think of them as a company started in a garage.

I don't know why I would, they had been producing systems for some
years before the PC.

> After IBM saw how successful the PC was, it pulled the PC back into the corporate fold,
> which produced flops including the PC Jr (make it into toy),

The IBM PC (pre XT) had a cassette port, the PC Jr was just the same -
a toy. OK, it was a toy with a crap keyboard.

> 3270 PC (make it into a
> terminal)

and don't forget the XT/370 running VM/PC plus CMS.

> and PS2  (make it proprietary).

The PS/2 was much later, after the XT, after the AT. PS/2 was a
reasonable attempt to not be an obsolete toy and actually have a
modern architecture with MCA. Others decided that EISA was good
enough. It had to wait for PCI to make a machine that matched the MCA
based PS/2s.

> >The poor implementation of the BIOS and of MS-DOS meant that basic
> >operations, such as screen i-o were very slow and lacked
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> graphics was for CGA only.

That wasn't true. http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hercules_Graphics_Card
says it was "widely supported" which is how I recall it. GEM And
Windows 1 supported Hercules graphics as did many games (certainly
Sierra games), flight simulators and such as SuperCalc and Lotus.

From http://worldcadaccess.typepad.com/blog/2004/02/redeath_of_herc.html

"The HGC (Hercules graphics card) became so popular that all graphics
software, including CAD, provided at least two drivers: one for the
IBM CGA, and one for the Hercules HGC."

> To show it on a mono screen, I had to change its aspect ratio.
> It was a challenge to do that with animated games fast enough to avoid ghosting and
> noticable lag.

There were several CGA emulators that worked well:

http://www.sorgonet.com/8086/CGA_emulation/

> >Programmers used segment arithmetic on pointers to access data faster
> >and this led to code that was incompatible with better processors and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> why anyone would.

It was required to address a contiguous area > 64Kb. It was usual to
'normalise' pointers in C, and sometimes the library would do it for
you.

> >Intel had made a chip that was 'the best 8bit micro',
>
> No, it was clearly a 16 bit CPU.

No. The 8088 had an 8 bit bus. It was sold to IBM who were planning to
use the Z80 that was already used by the System 23 as being almost a
plug-in replacement. Certainly the 8088 has 16 bit instructions (it
was 8086 compatible), but so did the Z80.

The phrase is in quotes because that is what Intel called it at the
time.

> > IBM had made a
> >machine a few percent better than the Apple ][ (eg more memory, faster
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> I don't think IBM corporate had a clear vision of the PC's role. Some saw it as a toy,

Exactly.

> some as competition for their low end 52xx single user machines, some as a smart terminal.
> I saw it as a tiny mainframe.

And, no doubt, you saw a skateboard as a 'tiny Kenworth'.

> >The mistake was by the programmers who, impressed by the brand name,
> >thought they could use these for real work instead of using properly
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> operations. The PC won.

Whatever. I am sure that you got 75Mbyte/sec out of your 4.7MHz 8088,
probably just on the serial port.

> I then proceeded to write a multi-tasking application under MSDOS, with cooperative task
> switching in the application. Each PC ran four tasks; each task handled two modems -- one
> data and one voice/DTMF. We ran ten PCs talking to up to 40 users.

Co-operative huh, how useful. Actually it probably bypassed MS-DOS
entirely (except for disk) and bit banged the hardware. I did use the
odd PC with a RAMPage card and an Arnet 8 port serial card with
Concurrent-DOS 6 and that gave pre-emptive multi-tasking. The
advantage was that it could recover the disk access time (head move +
latency) for use by other tasks while many multi-taskers running on
top of MS-DOS had that time as lost because MS-DOS was not re-entrant.

> >> OS/2 was obviously written by MVS guys, because its internal structures used the same
> >> terminology as MVS internals. Its failure disproves the assertion that mainframe expertise
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> users; OS/2 developers were trying to replace MVS; other IBMers were pushing Linux.

Excuse me but no one was 'pushing Linux' when OS/2 was being sold -
different decades.
```

```
On Mon, 6 Oct 2008 23:59:23 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Oct 7, 4:25ï¿½pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

>> >The poor implementation of the BIOS and of MS-DOS meant that basic
>> >operations, such as screen i-o were very slow and lacked
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>IBM CGA, and one for the Hercules HGC."

The articles are wrong. I was in the middle of this, as author of the best of the TSRs --
MODEMGA -- and a prototype tester for Hercules. Yes, GEM, CAD, MSFS and Lotus came with
Hercules drivers, but the software many users were running was written in Basic and C,
which supported only CGA.  Sierra games definitely did NOT support Hercules else I
wouldn't have added a 'terminate and stay resident across boot' feature to support them.
They did not run under DOS, they booted their own OS.

I started like the other TSR authors -- copy B000 to B800 every clock tick. That took one
day to write. Then I saw problems. 18.2 times per second isn't fast enough. Need to
synchronize with the screen scan to avoid split images. Need to understand the Hercules
4-way interleve so frame transition doesn't look like a fade. Need to set BIOS equipment
flags so applications think there is a CGA installed. Need to adjust for different aspect
ratios so circles don't look like ellipses. Need to deal with proprietary PCs such as AT&T
6300. 

I spent two months polishing MODEMGA into a commercial program, then advertised it in PC
Magazine.  1,000 copies were sold for $89.95. You might be able to find the ad if you have
magazines from the late '80s. 

>> To show it on a mono screen, I had to change its aspect ratio.
>> It was a challenge to do that with animated games fast enough to avoid ghosting and
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>http://www.sorgonet.com/8086/CGA_emulation/

They were my competition. I tried them all. They did not work well. 

>> >The mistake was by the programmers who, impressed by the brand name,
>> >thought they could use these for real work instead of using properly
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>probably just on the serial port.

IBM salesmen like to talk about raw hardware speed. If the customer incorrectly concludes
they apply to his throughput, that's not the salesman's fault, he salesman never SAID
that.

The mainframe ran slower than a PC because of multiple layers of software overhead,
especially in VSAM. 

>> I then proceeded to write a multi-tasking application under MSDOS, with cooperative task
>> switching in the application. Each PC ran four tasks; each task handled two modems -- one
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>entirely (except for disk) and bit banged the hardware.

Of necessity. Neither DOS nor BIOS supported modems.

> I did use the
>odd PC with a RAMPage card and an Arnet 8 port serial card with
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>top of MS-DOS had that time as lost because MS-DOS was not re-entrant.

Hardware designers created DMA so software didn't have to wait for disk. The DOS operating
system made it unusable. Worse, there was a DOS Busy flag that couldn't be trusted. 

>> >> OS/2 was obviously written by MVS guys, because its internal structures used the same
>> >> terminology as MVS internals. Its failure disproves the assertion that mainframe expertise
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>different decades.

 Slackware and Debian Linux were released in 1993, Red Hat in 1994.  OS/2 Warp was
released in 1994.
```

```
QBASIC was introduced in 1991.

>> Sierra games definitely did NOT support Hercules

>> They did not run under DOS, they booted their own OS.

>Playing games on the toy were we ?

I didn't play games on the PC. When I worked at PCSG, which wrote operating systems, a
spreadsheet (Lucid) and memory managers, we used to buy and run games to get ideas for
better graphics. We were so impressed with Wing Commander I (1990) that we called the guys
who wrote it (Origin Systems, Austin) to congratulate them. We estimated development time
of  100 man-years, they said we were close.

>> >> OS/2 is a good example of infighting between IBM divisions. IBM's own PC marketing
>> >> division was selling PCs bundled ï¿½with Windows. They, along with Microsoft, targeted home
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>collapsed in 2001.

IBM's ThinkPad 600E has passed certification tests for running version 6.0 of Red Hat
Linux.
September 1999
http://news.cnet.com/2100-1040-216224.html&tag=mncol%3btxt

Techweb has a nice story on the new IBM commitment to Linux. IBM will apparently announce
a new "affordable" Netfinity with a fresh version of Linux on it out of the box.
December 1999
http://www.screaming-penguin.com/node/1385

Among the changes: the availability of SuSE Linux on select RS/6000 servers and Caldera
OpenLinux on A20 and T20 ThinkPads; WebSphere support for Linux; the introduction of a
software bundle for small businesses relying on Linux; and funding for Linux education and
certification.

Big Blue already offers Linux on Netfinity servers and has been testing it on the S/390.
June 2000
http://news.cnet.com/IBM-ups-Linux-support-with-laptops/2100-1001_3-241733.html?tag=mncol

The last release of OS/2 was in December 2001. IBM sold it until 2005.
```

```
Business Model 	Commercial
```

```
finally... after all these postings someone makes a reference to pick. 
on a 512k pc pick interfaced 5 hospital lab machines (async) to the 
mainframe (3270 emulation).  fast.  NO crashes.  the only time the pick 
machine went down was when an upgrade required it.  the machine i worked 
with had had no ipl in over two years.  on a pc.  nothing wrong with the 
hardware.  can't say the same with the other os's.
```

```
>expertise wasn't used?

No, that proves mainframe expertise WAS used.

>For a while at least, OS/2 was far superior to DOS.  Remember Microsoft
>making an enormous fuss over version 3.1 (??) being able to recover from
>program crashes?  It was done in by perception that it was far too gredy in
>reosurces - it needed 2-3 megs of memory, for heaven's sake!

How much memory did YOUR PC have in 1989? Mine had 4 meg. I remember reading Microsoft's
next generation operating system, NT, would require 32 meg and thinking they won't be able
to sell it to the masses for ten years. They started selling a stsripped down version in
1993 and replaced Win 9x with it in 2000. My ten year prediction had been right. Thing is,
Microsoft had cash flow from DOS and Win 9x for those ten years. 

OS/2 failed because it wasn't pushed into the marketplace, bundled with PCs like DOS and
Windows. Users had to BUY it and install it. Toward the end they gave it away free, even
put CDs in some magazine. It was too little, too late.
```

```
 PH>   ---------------------------

in your case procedure division using link-area.
```

```


"Robert Broughton" <rbronews2@broug8hton.ca> wrote in message 
news:gcl6sl$pdk$1@venn.venn.bc.ca...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> Robert/Fumin bin Lyin has the bad habit of calling people liars.

Bob, I know this is irritating. There was a time when I used to get very 
unwrapped when someone did this to me, especially when I knew it wasn't the 
case. (I try very hard not to deliberately lie because the results of doing 
so are just too complex for my aging brain to keep up with.)

However, Usenet and the Internet are places where we expose ourselves to all 
kinds of abuse.

It really isn't a place for sensitivity. (I know this, because in real life 
I am quite sensitive and have been hurt by posts here on a number of 
occasions.) But even in negative experiences there is growth. It has been a 
long time since I was actually hurt by posts here, although I do get stung 
occasionally... :-)

The answer is not to respond in kind but to calmly and dispassionately ask 
for the reasoning behind the accusation.

(Considered grown-up response, rather than childish indulgenece of the 
immediate emotional reaction...)

Someone calls you a liar. Did you lie? If you didn't, state that you didn't 
and ask for the reasoning that led them to this conclusion. If you have 
evidence supporting your assertion, produce it. (Unfortunately, on one 
famous occasion here with one particular person who called me a liar, even 
sending him the evidence privately so as not to publicly humiliate him, was 
not enough to get him to withdraw his stupid statement. Sometimes people say 
things in anger that they really can't support and then, having painted 
themselves into a corner it is impossible for them to back down. Eventually, 
you just have to let it go.)

If you did lie, then accept that what is being said is the truth, but point 
out in your own defence that you do not habitually do so and on this 
occasion there was probably stress or some other extenuating circumstance. 
Most of us can relate to people being Human.

I don't know the circumstances under which Robert called you a liar, but I 
can't believe he would do so unprovoked.

Don't take it to heart. Responding by pillorying someone on a public web 
site will not win people to your cause.

> while, I was posting the list of his lies in response when he did this.

While that is a playground response, it is the one that most of us would 
naturally go with... :-)

"You're a liar"
"No, you're a liar"
"No, you're a liar."

As if showing that the other person lies has any bearing on your own 
fabrication :-)

> Adding the list to http://fumingmad.com/ was just a matter of convenience
> and brevity. It's easier to just post the URL every time instead of 
> posting
> the list.

The question I would ask is: "What does such a list achieve?".

OK, it helps you vent your anger, but do you think anyone is actually 
persuaded by it?

In fact, a fair-minded neutral observer (like me... :-)) is more likely to 
consider it a cheap shot than to avidly rush into agreement with the 
sentiments expressed.

The internet is an irritating place sometimes, and there are definitely 
people we would rather not communicate with. You have a perfect right to 
choose who you are OK with and who you are not, but you will win more 
support by being fair and open than you will by sniping from cover.

If you have an issue with Robert (or anyone else...) the best course is to 
state it and see what the response is. You might be surpprised at the 
result.  More importantly, it is good practise for learning to settle 
differences in a way that is acceptable, grown-up, and leaves you with your 
dignity intact.

Flaming is for kids who don't know better; adults resolve differences like 
men... (And that doesn't always require pistols at dawn...)

Whern all is said and done, you don't have to like Robert, or even have any 
truck with him at all.

But your present course actually diminishes you more than it harms him.

Think on it.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: date processing in z/OS

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>

```
Frank,
  Have you looked at using "environment variables"?  These can be set via LE 
run-time option and then queried within a COBOL program.  If you have any 
questions on how to do this, let me know (on or off-list).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: date processing in z/OS

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2008-10-01T17:45:15-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<48E3B72B.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<48DD3200.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <HJadnXC5cL8nFkPVnZ2dnUVZ_tbinZ2d@earthlink.com> <48E11D33.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <46QEk.210298$DI2.106177@fe02.news.easynews.com>`

```
>>> On 10/1/2008 at 1:22 PM, in message
<46QEk.210298$DI2.106177@fe02.news.easynews.com>, William M.
Klein<wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
> Frank,
>   Have you looked at using "environment variables"?  These can be set 
> via LE 
> run-time option and then queried within a COBOL program.  If you have any

> 
> questions on how to do this, let me know (on or off-list).

I think this might be the answer!  I knew about CEEENV, but I yesterday I
was not able to find out how to set them in the JCL.  But now that you have
triggered my thought process I was able to find it.

So how's this for some JCL:

//CEEENV2  JOB '',FRANK,CLASS=A,MSGCLASS=X,NOTIFY=&SYSUID           
//JOBLIB   DD DISP=SHR,DSN=FJS.PDS.LOAD                             
//STEP1    EXEC PGM=CEEENV1,PARM='/RPTOPTS(ON)'
//CEEOPTS  DD *                                                     
ENVAR(BUSDATE=20081230)                                             
//SYSIN    DD *                                                     
BUSDATE                                                             
//                                                                  

The program CEEENV1 calls the CEEENV routine and retrieves the environment
variable called BUSDATE.  Works like a charm!

I think I have to set the ENVAR using the DD:CEEOPTS method, rather than the
PARM method, because we have some IMS job and if it's anything like VSE's
DL/I we can't use the PARM clause on the EXEC card to specify LE runtime
options, because the program that actually executes is the IMS (or DLI)
execute driver, and it is not (under VSE) LE-enabled.  Haven't actually
tried it under z/OS, so perhaps it will work, but I think the CEEOPTS is the
better way to go, since I can just have something like:

Member FJS.PDS.JCL(BUSDATE)
//CEEOPTS  DD DISP=SHR,DSN=FJS.PDS.COBOL(BUSDATE)

Member FJS.PDS.COBOL(BUSDATE):
ENVAR(BUSDATE=20081230)

Member FJS.PDS.JCL(CEEENV2):
//CEEENV2  JOB '',FRANK,CLASS=A,MSGCLASS=X,NOTIFY=&SYSUID
//JOBLIB   DD DISP=SHR,DSN=FJS.PDS.LOAD                  
//         JCLLIB ORDER=FJS.PDS.JCL                      
//STEP1    EXEC PGM=CEEENV1,PARM='/RPTOPTS(ON)'          
//SYSIN    DD *                                          
BUSDATE                                                  
//         INCLUDE MEMBER=BUSDATE                        
//                                                       

I don't see any flaws, so I think I will go this way.  Thanks Bill!!!

Frank
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: date processing in z/OS

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Joel C. Ewing" <jcewing@acm.org>
- **Date:** 2008-10-09T12:08:04-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0dmdnUniQbvpp3PVnZ2dnUVZ_rHinZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<Ij9Fk.292730$vn1.27362@fe03.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<48DD3200.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <HJadnXC5cL8nFkPVnZ2dnUVZ_tbinZ2d@earthlink.com> <48E11D33.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <46QEk.210298$DI2.106177@fe02.news.easynews.com> <48E3B72B.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <Ij9Fk.292730$vn1.27362@fe03.news.easynews.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> Yes, I agree that the CEEOPTS DD is the way to go (with either inline data or 
> pointing to a PDS member).
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

I have always believed the main reason MVS didn't consider adding 
similar support for run-time date alteration to be a Security/Auditing 
issue (not a major consideration in the design of DOS).

If a program is written to ask the system the current date, which could 
then be used to generate logging information, control dates in corporate 
financial records, or possibly even to make decisions as to whether 
requested actions are reasonable, then should it be acceptable that a 
trivial JCL change could make the program run with a "bogus" date 
without that having been part of the deliberate intent and design of the 
program?

Since there are ways to supply a program with alternative dates for 
processes that have a legitimate need for run dates different than 
current date, it makes sense that a program should also be able to 
request a current date with confidence that it will actually receive a 
current date for those case where use of true current date is either 
expected or a legal requirement.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: date processing in z/OS

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2008-10-06T12:11:55-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<48EA008B.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<48DD3200.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <HJadnXC5cL8nFkPVnZ2dnUVZ_tbinZ2d@earthlink.com> <48E11D33.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <46QEk.210298$DI2.106177@fe02.news.easynews.com> <48E3B72B.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
I just want to add a bit to this thread, just in case others utilize it...

When I tried using CEEOPTS from within an IMS program on Friday *it did not
work*!  And man was I pissed!  But I did a bit of searching an I found the
following APAR:

http://www-01.ibm.com/support/docview.wss?uid=isg1PK58482

Here's the title of the APAR:
PK58482: CEEOPTS IGNORED WHEN RUNNING DB2 STORED PROCEDURES WRITTEN IN PL/I
AS THE SOURCE INCLUDES NOEXECOPS

Now, I am using neither PL/I nor DB2, but it seemed slightly promising. 
Within the body of the APAR it says something about a previous restriction
in for IMS using DD:CEEOPTS is removed with this APAR, which is exactly what
I was looking for.

So we got it ordered and applied today, and guess what.  It works!  Thank
god someone already ran it to this and got this APAR going, because it
really does seem to me to be the way to go, and I can't imagine a good
reason why it shouldn't work.  I think it just hadn't been considered.

Anyway, now I'm off to make good use of it.

Frank
```

## Thread: OT : NZ Lamb — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT : NZ Lamb

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2008-10-04T13:42:21-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<wGPFk.19$Oc2.10@newsfe05.iad>`

```
Pete,

Been wanting to ask this for ages, and while at Co-Op getting some milk 
jotted down lamb prices. Unfortunately ball-point pens have made my 
writing horrible, so I scribbled down prices/descriptions and even have 
difficulty reading my own writing back.

(Borrowed a ball point and got a a scrap of cardboard from an English 
guy filling the veggie shelves. "Gor blimey". He was amazed when I told 
him one of my mother's sisters lived at 111 Calaibria Road, Highbury 
N.London 4 or 5 decades ago. "Strewth, my dad only lives a few doors 
from there".)

As you'll recall years ago I told you we had bumper stickers for cars 
with "Eat Alberta Lamb. 30,000 coyotes can't be wrong". Being meat, 
(beef) and potato eaters, Albertans never took to lamb, and it is only 
within the last year or so that we have had Alberta lamb on display. 
Where does it go - from a recent leaflet, 80% to Quebec.

Anyway the prices, bearing in mind US and Canadian dollars are only a 
few cents apart at the moment.

Price		Amount		Description
$ per Kg	$

15.41		44.10		AB Leg Shank
?		26.51		NZ Lamb Leg (forgot to note price)
18.11		39.30		NZ Fresh Lamb Rack
15.41		26.51		NZ Lamb Leg Shank P????

Last one looks weird. I pointed out to the butcher it was the same price 
per Kg as the Albertan. He said somebody must have made a mistake on the 
ticketing; he went back and checked. Price was correct.

I'd love to know how many pennies your NZ farmers make out of this deal. 
Sure prices have zoomed, (primarily oil), but that has affected you as 
much as us. Then of course one must take into consideration the standard 
of living per country differs.

Salaries, I'm way out of whack not being in the market place. Marketing 
natural gas I guesstimated my son would make say $60K p.a - never have 
and wouldn't ask him. Initially about three months back saw an article 
in the Calgary Herald, can't remember the topic, but it started "A 
working couple living in Bridgeland, earning $76K pa .......". Wow ! I 
wish - I'd be as happy as a pig in shit if I got a quarters of that !

Doggy-people, met a bank employee, head office not a teller. "Rubbish", 
she said, "Two semi-professionals. Try $100K". Then I think back a 
couple of years. Somebody back East. Worked for a company taken over by 
a US firm. They really were doing nothing; and he was both bored and 
embarrassed to take their money. So he, the only programmer and five 
others went off and started their own computer company. Two years ago he 
walked away from a programming job, outskirts of Toronto, paying him 
$100K pa.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------

It makes sense for me to give up on computing when I did. Worked friggin 
hard and earning zero.

I gave you the details years ago and a reference I made in here CLC, you 
commented "Ahh the killer application that we all dream about".

Well we had it, not complicated program-wise but associated with 
Engineering you  had to understand the buzzwords and formulae. A branch 
of NDT (Non-Destructive Testing) it was an ultrasonic probe application. 
By law, in the oil/gas/chemical, engineering world, probably 
universally, governments both local and federal demand that on an annual 
basis you submit returns on the state of production equipment - 
specifically vessels (like you would visualize a home boiler, pipes 
going into and out of tanks with bends in the pipes. The agency in 
Alberta is called the Boiler's Branch. You are particularly looking for 
corrosion in the pipe bends; sulphur content automatically comes to 
mind, but just plain water is the main killer. (The water builds up a 
'sand-bank' like in a river and the contents of that sediment is what 
causes the piping to corrode.

Let's call him by his name starting with "K". He was a very smart young 
man, I dubbed him a "computer freak". He was always very pleasant but 
very demanding, looking forward light years ahead of where I was 
currently programming. He visualised the day he could transmit the data 
from his van in the boonies via satellite back to his office in Calgary, 
so it could be worked on while he was still out in the field. Give me a 
break ! Me, a one-man outfit, concentrating on getting a comfortable 
handle on OO - which I have now achieved - I didn't even have time to 
concern myself with programming for the Web. That would have come later, 
and hopefully his satellite idea 'Never'.

The actual application - way, way north of Edmonton, K is sat in the 
back of his van powered by a diesel battery. He sits in the van, an 
office area, ultrasonic screen and PC in front of him. Communicates with 
the helper where to go next. The likely corrosion spots on a pipe are 
already marked (called points). The tecchie holds a probe against each 
point, which transmits an imperial 99.999 inches reading back to his 
ultrasonic screen in the van. The graph can be a bit iffy so he may 
adjust the reading slightly - that he keys into the dialog on the PC. 
Dialog is two-dimensional 10 columns (allowing for 10 inspection dates, 
usually 12 months apart, unless a user requests more frequent readings 
on dodgy equipment); 25 rows/lines allowing for a maximum of 25 points 
on a pipe - averages around 10 points and sometimes goes to 15.

Having stored the data, do a standard set of calculations so that 
reports can be printed showing the Life cycle of Vessels ( indicated by 
the Items in the report, i.e. individual pipes ) comparing one date set 
of readings to the next, showing the gradual loss due to corrosion until 
it hits Nominal Critical level (pre-defined from engineering tables or 
formulae in the program). The reports in terms of Life cycle are 
ASCENDING by number of years calculated showing the first likely Vessels 
to fail. (If they get a Vessel go kaput, oil companies can be talking 
$millions, daily, until the Vessel unit is fixed/exchanged to bring it 
back on stream in the production process).

First engineer K ever approached said, "Of course this is written in 
FORTRAN ?". That's what they used to teach them back at school :-)
Unfortunately K used to keep the tech stuff close to his chest, only 
feeding me the tech detail as necessary - I suppose thinking I would go 
off and do my own thing. Well off and on through two companies and the 
period 1980 - 2000, working on Datapoint's DATABUS, RM/COBOL on a Tandy 
Radio Shack Model II, and having 'plugged' the Tandy machine to a Comapq 
portable we got it into DOS using a a PC-based version of RM/COBOL.

The money I was getting was iffy - it was his lawyer advised him 
ethically he should be giving me a regular monthly sum. K the 'Mr. 
techno-freak', was always looking to the future and got into the Web. 
"Try this, try that.....". I had between times switched to MS/COBOL and 
then Bill G pointed us at Micro Focus - who were they ? So bought M/F 
and and started re-programming the application in text mode. Not content 
with using the Web as a general source, he's looking at COBOL, 'cos he 
wants "Winders". Very first one, look at CA-Realia, then while I'm 
absorbing that, Fujitsu - couldn't possibly tell you which version - it 
was segmented into two parts and the compiler progression was 
illustrated by a clock thinggy. The manuals, when you printed them, said 
you could use any Editor. No luck there, e-mailed them and got back zip. 
Then he spotted some outfit he didn't know about called Micro Focus - 
they had a beta (VISOC) which did "Winders". I liked and was comfortable 
with their DOS version so that's the route I went. OMG ! Object 
Orientation ?????? The rest is history.

Snag is, all the while I am absorbing the various compilers and trying 
to get the hang of OO and GUI-ing, he's off on another tack - here's an 
enhancement he wants or claims a user 'demands to have it, like 
yesterday'. Put on the brakes, turn your head around 180 degrees and get 
back to the RM/COBOL all UPPERCASE (Uggh !), no END terminators - we are 
talking COBOL 74 - and no slick Animator to Debug. Sure you could get 
into DEBUG mode with RM, but in machine code or whatever - not this 
Johnnie's scene. And this process of switching from M/F back to RM for 
quick fixes just went on for years and years. I had to cycle re-setting 
my brain into RM or MF mode as appropriate. We both got pissed off, he 
was paying money getting no result and I was truly frustrated with the 
constant switching. No haranguing, but we parted company in 2000 after I 
came back from J4 held at Newbury. Never spelled out but I think he 
objected that I took that first holiday in 20-odd years !

So yes at this point I did do my own thing, continuing to write the 
application in OO. There were always factors floating in the back of my 
mind - no technical contact, how was I to know if there were any 
significant changes in NDT, plus a guess and probably very likely, the 
collection techniques have changed using new equipment. Example, oil 
pipelines trundling across the countryside getting oil say from Alberta 
to Chicago. They too have to be inspected for corrosion - but they have 
used for years little mini-carts/trains/machines traveling automatically 
along the pipes taken thousands, perhaps zillions of readings. I'll bet 
the equipment used at static oil/gs plants have adapted to this 
technology, certainly making a considerable change to the input to a 
computer system, plus the volumes and volumes of additional readings 
generated. (For years K has been using Kraut-Kramer instruments from 
(Sweden or Denmark) when it necessitates taking the top off a huge 
storage vessel - becomes a bit 'destructive' as opposed to 
'non-destructive' at that point - set the machine on the rim of the 
vessel top and it automatically moves around taking a considerable 
number of readings and from the results does calculations, graphs etc.)

No engineering expertise or backup, then reality really sets in. Be OK 
if they don't meet me, (i.e. did the marketing via the Web), but 
inevitably someone would ask me to come downtown. In walks this 
white-haired old gent of 77 years; even worse the poor old sod is using 
a walking cane, because of the hip surgery. We can't deal with him, 
he'll croak anytime soon !

I've just recapped above and see I missed the point about the 'killer 
application' - Way back as I switched to MF OO, K had a very detailed 
enquiry from Singapore about NDT software. Needless to say K wanted to 
jump in. I had to put him off, we just weren't ready. If you do a google 
on NDT I think you might *perhaps* find one reference to Ultrasonic 
software relating to production plants. K and myself, we are both aware 
of some applications written in DB access languages and they are 
dreadful - systems design - not programming - they have no concept of a 
hierarchical structure on identifying keys and require a lot of 
explaining before you can 'nurse' the applications. Can't remember 
whether it was Chevron or Pan-Canadian - they brought in an office 
worker just to feed data into the system because it was so complicated. 
Not necessarily $millions but I think we could have both been content 
with $150 - 300K per annum :-)

There's really no joy in programming just as a hobby - not unless you 
are an idiot. You young 'uns out there in your forties, be glad you have 
a job in the corporate world.

Jimmy
```

#### ↳ OT Re: NZ Lamb

```
"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:wGPFk.19$Oc2.10@newsfe05.iad...
> Pete,
>
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> I'd love to know how many pennies your NZ farmers make out of this deal.

They do very nicely. NZ is one of the most efficient lamb producers in the 
world thanks to over a century of study, applied science and hard work by 
Kiwi farmers. The first shipments of frozen NZ lamb were sent to the U.K. on 
sailing ships in the 1880s, so we've been doing it for a while.

We can raise lambs, slaughter them, snap freeze them, and ship them anywhere 
in the world for less than the cost to local farmers in most of the world.

However, the sheep (particularly wool) industry has declined from a peak of 
over 70 million sheep to somewhere around 40 million today.

We have the right terrain for sheep (both mountain and plains) and careful 
selective breeding over decades has ensured that the sheep we raise are 
ideally suited to the environments where they will live.

We even have Halal slaughterhouses facing East to provide for export to 
Islamic countries like Iran, which are a big market for our lamb. 
(Originally, they wanted live exports so they could slaughter the sheep on 
arrival, according to the requirements of their religion. It was decided 
this was too cruel as the journey by sea was several weeks and it was pretty 
hard on the sheep. The solution was to have them ritually slaughtered here 
then frozen and shipped. Much less hard on the sheep...:-))

As for prices, we have noticed a steady increase in the supermarkets here as 
most of the production is exported. Today, I would expect to pay around 
$US15.00 for a decent leg of lamb. I can buy half a sheep (butchered, packed 
into chops, cuts, leg, and frozen, we call them freezer packs and they would 
feed a family for several weeks) for around $US20.00 but that is not lamb; 
it is mutton.

It hasn't quite got to the point where lamb here is considered a luxury (as 
it certainly has in Europe), but it is edging away from the budget of most 
housewives.

"The mountain sheep are sweeter
  But the valley sheep are fatter
  We therefore deemed it meeter
   To carry off the latter."

From the "War song of Dinas Vawr"  by Thomas Love Peacock. (It is from his 
comic novel about the Welsh in Arthurian times "The misfortunes of Elphin" 
... I can recommend this as a ripping good yarn and very amusing.  Dinas 
Fawr in Welsh means "Big Fort" and was the stronghold (according to Peacock) 
of King Ednyfed until he was overthrown by King  Melvas, who beheaded him 
and took all his cattle and sheep. Anyone who has ever driven across the 
Severn bridge and been enchanted by the Wye valley and South Wales should 
find this interesting.)

Jimmy, if you re-read the above, it is a history of him wanting to do stuff

really no reason why you couldn't have investigated some of this stuff and 
seen what was involved. Instead, you stuck to what you knew and limited him 
to that too. No wonder it didn't work out.

When you say "we just weren't ready" what you really mean is YOU weren't 
ready. He saw an opportunity. He doesn't understand computer technology; 
that's what he's paying you for. So maybe you didn't have some of the 
necessary skills... that's no sin, people acquire skill "on-the-fly" all the 
time. Almost anyone who has worked as a contractor has been required to do 
stuff that isn't part of their job spec. I wouldn't mind a buck for every 
time in my career I've said: "Sounds interesting... I don't know much about 
this, but I'll find out what's involved and get back to you by Wednesday..." 
or similar. Sometimes it involved scripting, or spreadsheets, or stuff I 
claimed no particular expertise in at the time, (having been employed as  a 
contract COBOL programmer) but you want to deliver what is wanted (and it is 
always nice to learn something while someone is paying you...). Sometimes, 
what is involved really is too much to be done quickly. But at least if you 
have identified what's needed they can then decide whether they buy the 
skill for it this one time or they don't.  It's about making yourself 
valuable to whomever is paying you. You don't do that by just saying "no"...

You probably remember the old Unipart campaign in the U.K.: "The answer's 
'Yes', now, what's the question?" :-)

>Ultrasonic software relating to production plants. K and myself, we are 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…

Well, I guess I'm an idiot. I do a lot of programming just because I can and 
I enjoy it. Often it turns out to be worth something and I can generate 
enough cash from it to keep me in (good) wine, although I will need to do 
some more serious work before I can safely retire. At least my "hobby" 
allows me to spend the Summer here and not have to worry. :-) The recent 
exercise with the COBOL structure tool was done mainly for fun. The site has 
taken 500 hits, more than 20 people have downloaded the tool (and most of 
them were people I don't know and have never seen post here, so there ARE 
lurkers in CLC :-)), and I have had two serious enquiries for migration 
tools generated from this exercise. Interestingly, very few people had 
difficulty in unlocking the menus and downloading. Nevertheless, I am 
looking to change it when I have time, based on conversations and 
suggestions from this forum. My point is that "hobby programming" may not be 
immediately lucrative, but that's not why you do it. It is about 
satisfaction and pride of achievement, as well as money. And if it can help 
someone, why wouldn't you do it?



>You young 'uns out there in your forties, be glad you have a job in the 
>corporate world.
>

Ouch! I 'd say: "As long as you are working for someone else, you will 
rarely achieve financial independence. For every buck they pay you, you are 
making three for them (If you're not, they can't afford to pay you one...). 
The sooner you can build a skill set and sell it to the market place as a 
contractor, the better off you will be. Jobs for life are a thing of the 
past. They'll fire you at the drop of a hat if they need to. Take 
responsibility for your own future, relying on a corporation is the path to 
almost certain doom...."

However, I realize that this is strong stuff and not for the faint of heart. 
It won't suit everybody... Nevertheless, it has worked pretty well for me 
for over three decades now. I'm debt free (no mortgage, and credit cards 
paid up), live in a nice place, and the food is great :-) Life is good... 
Unfortunately, In order to ensure I can still flit off when I feel like it, 
and can still buy stuff without having to check the price tag, after I 
officially retire, I need to do one more stint in Europe, and will do so 
after the Summer here. I'm really getting too old for this kind of travel 
and it doesn't excite me the way it used to, but I can do it one more time. 
Might come back by sea... I reckon that could be interesting... :-)

Pete.
```

<a id="t-b4253393cb"></a>
## Thread: Dynamiv SQL and multi-row access SQL from COBOL... some quick questions — 5 msgs

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### Dynamiv SQL and multi-row access SQL from COBOL... some quick questions

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-10-08T23:22:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6l3g02Fafi2nU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Does anyone KNOW (not just think... I can do THAT myself... :-)) whether a 
dynamic SQL statement has to be PREPARED EVERY time you use it, or can it be 
PREPARED once and, if the parameters don't change, can you EXECUTE it again, 
WITHOUT having to prepare it?

I have some fairly complex dynamic SQL and I think building it and preparing 
every time I use it is going to be an overhead I don't really want. I can 
"remember" the variable bits and check whether they've changed, in which 
case I need to rebuild and PREPARE it again, but if they havent I'd like to 
just EXECUTE the same statement that was already PREPARED.

Secondly I've had some spectacular failures with SQL multi-row access from 
COBOL using ACCESS 2003.

Consider a table with 6 rows and 3 columns.... (It is a table attached to a 
main table and contains 6 currency figures)

The table is called: ARPMF0-RECrg01  where the "rg01" indicates it was 
generated to support the first repeating group encountered in the original 
COBOL data definition.

the columns are:  ARPMF0-CD  *> primary key of the master record
                          ARPMF0-BAL *> a currency balance
                          LEVEL-J          *> an index that ranges from 1 to 
6 for any given ARPMF0-CD value

ARPMF0-CD and Level-J together are the primary key of this table.

The table has been generated in response to a COBOL 'OCCURS 6' In the 
orginal ISAM data definition.

Of course, by increasing the value of LEVEL-J, any number of balances can be 
attached to a given master key, and splitting
out the repeating groups enables proper normalization to at least 2NF.


Here are the Host Variable definitions, designed to allow multi-row 
access...

001945
001946* HOST VARIABLE definitions for table: ARPMF0-RECrg01
001947* Generated by MOSTGEN on: 13-Nov-2007   at: 10:21:26.89
001948*
001949* PRIMARY Key field(s): ARPMF0-CD, LEVEL-J
001950*
001951* This is a multi-row structure (table) generated by MOSTGEN
001952* from the actual DECLGEN. It allows multi-row SQL access
001953* although this has been found not to work for insert and update.
001954 01  HV-ARPMF0-RECrg01.
001955     12  HV-ARPMF0-CD-MULTI    pic x(8)             occurs 6. *> 
Master generic key
001956     12  HV-ARPMF0-BAL               pic s9(14)v9(4)  occurs 6. *> 
Currency field
001957     12  HV-ARPMF0-LEVEL-J        pic s99               occurs 6. *> 
primary key component


Here's some program code...

009284*
009285* NONE OF THIS 'ELEGANT' CODE WORKS!!!
009286* Compiles OK... but
009287*   (This should load the 6 rows with each of the 3 fields, using a 
single SQL statement)
009288*     set RG-x1 to 1
009289*     EXEC SQL
009290*       SELECT * FROM  [ARPMF0-RECrg01]
009291*         INTO  :HV-ARPMF0-RECrg01
009292*        WHERE  [ARPMF0-CD] = :HV-DynamicParm
009293*     END-EXEC
009294*

the balances. They are all shifted by 100.
009296**
009297*    (This should update the 6 rows on the table with a single 
statement...)

009299*        UPDATE [ARPMF0-RECrg01]
009300*          SET [ARPMF0-BAL] = :HV-ARPMF0-BAL
009301*          WHERE [ARPMF0-CD] = :HV-ARPMF0-CD-MULTI AND
009302*                [LEVEL-J]   = :HV-ARPMF0-LEVEL-J
009303*     END-EXEC
009304*
009305*...this multi-row update returns SQLSTATE S1C00 "Optional feature not 
implemented"
009306** Looks like MS ACCESS does not support multi-row update so we have 
to do
009307* it by brute force... only change the fields we need to.
009308* Let's see what's changed...
009309* This single select loads all 6 balances, correctly aligned... so we 
can compare with what we are trying to update
009310* Unfortunately it doesn't work with INSERT and UPDATE...
                (This is the only multi-row access that works exactly as it 
should...it even loads the key correctly 6 times and the values of LEVEL-J)
009311     set RG-x1 to 1
009312     EXEC SQL
009313       SELECT [ARPMF0-BAL]
009314         INTO  :HV-ARPMF0-BAL
009315         from [ARPMF0-RECrg01]
009316        WHERE [ARPMF0-CD] = :HV-DynamicParm
009317     END-EXEC
009318     move SQLERRD (3) to RG-noloaded (RG-x1) *> returns the number of 
rows loaded
009319

I checked out the SQL trace and found that Fujitsu COBOL Version 6 generates 
deprecated SQL to handle Parameters for the INSERT and UPDATE calls, and 
that is where the process fails...

There is very little I can do about that except scrub round it...

If anyone has had any experience with multi-row INSERT/UPDATE and can 
suggest an alternative construct, I'd be glad to hear it. I tried everything 
I could think of.

If I were doing this for myself, in C#, it would be a multi-row resultset 
through OLE or ODBC and none of these problems would arise. Unfortunately, 
it isn't for me and has to be in COBOL...

Cheers,

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Dynamiv SQL and multi-row access SQL from COBOL... some quick questions

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-10-08T08:41:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<02cpe4he45h8ptt42n3ntt0mua667ho8sm@4ax.com>`

```
On Wed, 8 Oct 2008 23:22:55 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>Does anyone KNOW (not just think... I can do THAT myself... :-)) whether a 
>dynamic SQL statement has to be PREPARED EVERY time you use it, or can it be 
>PREPARED once and, if the parameters don't change, can you EXECUTE it again, 
>WITHOUT having to prepare it?

Yes, a PREPAREd statement can be EXECUTEd multiple times. That's why it has a handle. 

EXECUTE IMMEDIATE is for one time dynamic SQL. 

>I have some fairly complex dynamic SQL and I think building it and preparing 
>every time I use it is going to be an overhead I don't really want. I can 
>"remember" the variable bits and check whether they've changed, in which 
>case I need to rebuild and PREPARE it again, but if they havent I'd like to 
>just EXECUTE the same statement that was already PREPARED.

A PREPAREd statement can and should use bind variables rather than literals. Remember that
the statement executes on the server, which doesn't have access to the client's memory,
thus the EXECUTE must pass values. That's done differently on each database. On Oracle,
names in the PREPARE are meaningless positional tokens, replacement is done by counting. 
Read the manual.

I tried to warn you about this a month ago. 


>
>Secondly I've had some spectacular failures with SQL multi-row access from 
…[60 more quoted lines elided]…
>the balances. They are all shifted by 100.

Of course they're shifted, because you did not explicitly name the fields. 
You're counting on the precompiler to expand [ARPMF0-RECrg0] into a list of components,
and on the server to expand * into a list of columns. The expansion of * is too late for
the precompiler to do a type conversion from number to Cobol type.

SELECT * in embedded SQL is an amateurish no-no. There is no excuse laziness in generated
code. 

>009296**
>009297*    (This should update the 6 rows on the table with a single 
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>I could think of.

Try doing it inside a T-SQL loop. 

>If I were doing this for myself, in C#, it would be a multi-row resultset 
>through OLE or ODBC and none of these problems would arise. Unfortunately, 
>it isn't for me and has to be in COBOL...

COBOL has nothing to do with it. This is a SQL problem.
```

```
> laziness in generated

Tell Fujitsu.  Besides, I've been writing SELECT * for a number of years 
without problem. (Admittedly, I don't do it that often - because it means 
fetching every column - but for tables with a small number of columns (like 
this one), and where I need all of them, I see no reason not to do it. It is 
an efficient and time-saving shorthand (I wonder if that's why they included 
it in SQL...?)What specifically makes you think it is amateurish? Don't just 
hand down a lofty verdict, support your case.

The fact that this code is being generated by other code I wrote does mean I 
COULD reference every column. But until the construct works, I won't be 
generating it, and that means I have to test it by hand...

>
>>009296**
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> Try doing it inside a T-SQL loop.

That's a good suggestion. I'm actually doing it inside a COBOL loop. I only 
update changed balances. But a triggered procedure might solve the problem 
equally well and it then becomes a background process that is no longer my 
responsibility... :-)
>
>>If I were doing this for myself, in C#, it would be a multi-row resultset
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL has nothing to do with it. This is a SQL problem.

No, it isn't. COBOL generates a PREPARE and Parameters for these multi row 
update constructs, and passes these low-level calls to the ODBC driver. They 
simply don't work because the call being used is deprecated and should be 
replaced by a modern equivalent.

The offending call is about setting the parameters for the generated 
PREPARE. It SHOULD use a "BIND Parameters" type call, and it uses an 
earlier, now deprecated, form instead. The result is that the call it does 
use has a zero parameter address, causing it to return -1 and raising the 
SQLSTATE S1C00. (This is a bit of a "catch-all" and has little to do with 
options not being implemented.)

I have no control over the actual SQL calls generated, but I have watched 
the traces and know what it does.

It isn't an SQL problem. It is a COBOL problem because it is generating 
outdated calls. Possibly, using a later version of the compiler would solve 
it, but I don't have version 7 and I have no intention of getting it. I CAN 
work around this problem; I just thought there might be an alternative 
construct for multi-row access that could change the generated calls.

 If I used C# I wouldn't get these stupid, primitive low-level calls, I 
could return an entire result set with one call and disconnect from the DB, 
with subsequent processing carried out in memory, and the result set would 
automatically reconnect and update when I was finished.

OK, Thanks for your response. You confirmed one thing for me and made me 
think about another :-)

Pete.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<i8hqe4p7ec1o2bmh83qg0irkcmjm3el506@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<6l3g02Fafi2nU1@mid.individual.net> <02cpe4he45h8ptt42n3ntt0mua667ho8sm@4ax.com> <6l4q4fFalkpfU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Thu, 9 Oct 2008 11:22:12 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>
>
…[40 more quoted lines elided]…
>I took your warning on board. and am NOT using literals. 

You wrote " I can "remember" the variable bits and check whether they've changed, in which
case I need to rebuild and PREPARE it again, but if they havent I'd like  to
just EXECUTE the same statement that was already PREPARED." 

That sounds like you put literal values in SQL. If you are using host variables, there is
no reason to rebuild and PREPARE when values change. 

Perhaps you were referring to something other than host variables. 

>I understand 
>exactly how it works after spending several hours going through SQL traces. 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>anyway.

I meant the syntax for defining and passing parameters in dynamic SQL varies, because it
was non-Standard in '92.

>>>Secondly I've had some spectacular failures with SQL multi-row access from
>>>COBOL using ACCESS 2003.
…[81 more quoted lines elided]…
>Hmmm...maybe... It seems to do it OK when there is only one row involved....

In that case, it does sound like a precompiler bug. 

The preferred syntax is 

  SELECT CD, BAL, LEVEL_J
    FROM  [ARPMF0-RECrg01]
    INTO 
      : HV-ARPMF0-CD-MULTI,
     : HV-ARPMF0-BAL,
     :HV-ARPMF0-LEVEL-J       
    WHERE CD = :HV-DynamicParm

I would expect that to work where the abbreviated form failed. 

>> SELECT * in embedded SQL is an amateurish no-no. There is no excuse 
>> laziness in generated
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>hand down a lofty verdict, support your case.

The reason is future changes in table structure. If someone adds a column to the table,
programs will all stop working until host variable structures and code are regenerated. 

Database structure should not be coupled to programs. Adding a column should not affect
running programs, they just won't see the new column. 

For the same reason, the new column should not have a NOT NULL constraint without a
default value. If it does, old programs will fail on INSERT. 

>The fact that this code is being generated by other code I wrote does mean I 
>COULD reference every column. But until the construct works, I won't be 
>generating it, and that means I have to test it by hand...

Big shops handle this by reading the data dictionary (in the database) and generating two
source code files (per language), one "select list" containing column names, the other
"variable list" containing host variable names. This is run by the Make file. The SQL then
looks like

 SELECT
    INCLUDE arpmf0_select_list
    INTO 
    INCLUDE arpmf0-variable-list
    WHERE CD = :HV-DynamicParm

Prefixes like yours are handled with COPY REPLACING.

    01 ()--RECrg01.
        12 ()-CD-MULTI    pic x(8).

     COPY  arpmf0-variable-list REPLACING ==()== BY ==:HV-ARPFM0==.

>>>009296**
>>>009297*    (This should update the 6 rows on the table with a single
…[50 more quoted lines elided]…
>update changed balances.

A brute force approach is to dynamically construct an INSERT like this:

INSERT [ARPMF0-RECrg01] VALUES 
 (' : HV-ARPMF0-CD-MULTI(1)', ': HV-ARPMF0-BAL(1)', ':HV-ARPMF0-LEVEL-J(1)'),   
 (' : HV-ARPMF0-CD-MULTI(2)', ': HV-ARPMF0-BAL(2)', ':HV-ARPMF0-LEVEL-J(2)'),   ...

> But a triggered procedure might solve the problem 
>equally well and it then becomes a background process that is no longer my 
>responsibility... :-)

You would need a stored procedure that accepts a Cobol array and returns a SQL variable of
type table. Then you could INSERT table (col, col)  EXEC your_procedure(@param)

Given that  Fujitsu can pass a Cobol array to an EXEC of a prepared SELECT, the above has
a good chance of working.
```

```
comparison relation (>, =, < , for example...) I can't put that into a host 
variable, so I build it dynamically. However if the relation is the same and 
it is the same key of reference, then I don't need to re-PREPARE the 
statement. So I "remember" the last used relation and key of reference. (KOR 
data is set to a host variable and this is a parameter to the dynamic SQL.

Ah, more good stuff...
>
>>The fact that this code is being generated by other code I wrote does mean 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> "variable list" containing host variable names.

Now, it's really funny you should say that... :-) I came to exactly that 
conclusion this afternoon. I haven't yet decided where to source the two 
lists from. I COULD  get it from the system structure tables in the DB (the 
"dictionary" you suggest), but I also have a metadata file which is 
generated when the tables are created. This has more information on it, like 
what is OCCURring and what is REDEFINED and, as of quite recently, includes 
lengths and offsets :-), so I can easily lift Key of Reference contents from 
a data buffer and place them in a Host Variable, without being too concerned 
about the data contents and structure. Remember, my goal here is to maintain 
a COBOL record layout (which has structures, REDEFINES, and OCCURS on it), 
from a database which has none of these things. That record layout is the 
interface between my data access layer (DAL) and the applications. The 
applications need to function exactly as they always have, except that the 
data source has become an RDB instead of an ISAM file. It's the first step 
in a Migration process which is described on the COBOL structure tool 
website.



This is run by the Make file. The SQL then
> looks like
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes, I want to avoid INCLUDE and COPY.  As the MOSTGEN program has to write 
the entire code for the DAL Class that handles this table set, there is no 
advantage to me in using COPY or INCLUDE. (And there is a DISadvantage 
inasmuch as I have to set and enforce Libraries and Library paths on sites I 
really have no control over.)  I can simply generate the code into the 
program anyway. There will only ever be ONE data access Class that uses 
these two lists (for any given ISAM file/Table set) and if anyone was to 
modify the underlying ISAM or table set they would have to regenerate the 
MOST handler, anyway. The MOSTGEN program is being written in C# so it may 
be updated automatically and I may want changes in the way the lists are 
generated. For example, a DATE on the RDB has to be mapped against a PIC 
9(6) field in the record layout. I can't just move it to a Host variable.

The 'Cstr' function, combined with the IIF and IsNULL functions, converts 
the RDB date to a 10 character string as dd/mm/yyyy (it is kind of an ACCESS 
equivalent  to COALESCE) if it is valid, or returns '01/01/1900' if it 
isn't.

009620*  Date type field
009621*
009622     "IIF (NOT 
IsNull([ARPMF0-CREATED-ON]),CStr([ARPMF0-CREATED-ON]),'01/01/1900'),"
009623

The above would be part of the "field list" and, if it is in a SELECT it 
will correspond to a Host Variable of 10 bytes defined by INTO. which would 
be in the "HV list"...


I then reformat that into the 6 digits (or 8 or 10 - have to check metadata 
during code generation) required by the COBOL record layout...

ARPMF0-CREATED-ON is the field in the ISAM record layout that we are using 
as an interface, not the field on the database...

007863         *> date field
007864         move 10 to srcLength
007865         move 6 to tgtLength
007866         move 0  to outfmt8
007867         move HV-ARPMF0-CREATED-ON to src
007868         invoke SELF "DateHandler"
007869                using srcLength
007870                      src
007871                      tgtLength
007872                      tgt
007873                      outfmt8
007874         end-invoke
007876         compute ARPMF0-CREATED-ON = function NUMVAL (tgt)
007877         *>move   HV-ARPMF0-CREATED-ON         to  ARPMF0-CREATED-ON

As you can see (and I haven't even shown what has to be done to get it back 
to the RDB format,  if you're going the other way...
:-)), the "lists" are far from simple and that's another reason I don't want 
to use COPY and/or INCLUDE.   All of the above is generated on the fly by 
MOSTGEN.

The code in the DateHandler method is about 400 lines of COBOL which checks 
for all possible date formats in the record layout and adapts the contents 
so that the COBOL program sees what it expects, and the database sees a 
valid Date type.

It is a lot of work but is running correctly  and the process has stopped 
crashing because of invalid dates... the COBOL system just moved zero or 
spaces to the date field if it wasn't using it. This was disastrous when 
trying to load the RDB or manipulate the data on it.

>Prefixes like yours are handled with COPY REPLACING.

Yes, normally. Not in this case.
>
>    01 ()--RECrg01.
…[66 more quoted lines elided]…
> ':HV-ARPMF0-LEVEL-J(2)'),   ...

I figured there's enough dynamic SQL in the process already... :-)

Here's my "brute force" approach...

009321     perform
009322        varying J
009323           from 1
009324             by 1
009325          until J > 6
009326             if ARPMF0-BAL (J) NOT = HV-ARPMF0-BAL (J)
009327                move ARPMF0-BAL (J) to HV-ARPMF0-BAL-SINGLETON
009328                move J to HV-J
009329                EXEC SQL
009330                  UPDATE [ARPMF0-RECrg01]
009331                    SET [ARPMF0-BAL] = :HV-ARPMF0-BAL-SINGLETON
009332                  WHERE [LEVEL-J]    = :HV-J AND
009333                        [ARPMF0-CD]  = :HV-DynamicParm
009334                END-EXEC
009335                *> SQLSTATE SQLMSG
009336             end-if
009337     end-perform

Usually there is only one changed balance. As the record has previously been 
accessed  with a multi-row fetch, it is still buffered by the RDB so, 
overall, it isn't too bad. I increased the buffering in ACCESS and traced it 
and it ran to my satisfaction. Updates were deferred until the commit point 
as you would expect.


>
>> But a triggered procedure might solve the problem
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> your_procedure(@param)

That would be cool. I'll think more on this when the pressure's off. Right 
now I just want to get MOSTGEN completed.
>
> Given that  Fujitsu can pass a Cobol array to an EXEC of a prepared 
> SELECT, the above has
> a good chance of working.

Thanks for your thoughts, Robert. I found this helpful.

Cheers,

Pete.
```

## Thread: Linkage Section variables — 7 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Linkage Section variables

- **From:** Pakku <pakku@soccermail.com>
- **Date:** 2008-10-08T06:37:25-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d7abdb6a-42f1-4ae4-b409-b1d00eba3801@p31g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
I saw an existing discussion on this subject but can't seem to extend
it, so I am starting a new thread.  But here is the part which
interested me

==============================================================================
"charles leviton" <chuck_levi...@yahoo.com> wrote in message

news:d280a253.0306241210.5baa00fc@posting.google.com...

> Hi,
> I am primarily a CoolGEN developer using COBOL mostly to write
> routines that will read files and CALLing these from CoolGEN.

> Recently we encountered a situation where while stepping thru some
> CoolGEN code (ie running in trace mode) some variables lost their
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> away.

> So my question is what effect does it have when you initialize a
> variable that is in the linkage section?
> Thanks

The memory locations specified by the starting address + length of the
subprogram's named data item get set to blanks or zero. Suppose:

(calling program)
CALL 'A' USING X Y.  <== note two variables

(called program)
PROCEDURE DIVISION USING P Q R.  <== note subprogram expecting 3
variables
INITIALIZE P Q R.

You're screwed inasmuch as no starting address for "R" was passed by
the
calling program (so the called program had to wing it). The  blanks/
zeros
resulting from INITIALIZE could appear almost anywhere in memory (but
usually in the same place).

========================================================================

I am running into a problem where X, and Y are not declared next to
each other in WS.  As a result in the called program the value in P
matches to X but the value in Q is that of the variable in the WS that
was immediately below P

My WS looks like this
X PIC X(4)
W PIC X(10)
Y PIC X(8)

At the point of calling the subroutine, X = 'ABCD', W='2006-08-10',
Y='AUSERID'

When I step thru the subroutine, I find that Q has the value
'2006-08-' in it.

Is it a necessity that variables being passed into the subroutine
should be contiguous in the calling program?

'Cos when I made them contiguous, the problem went away!

Thanks
```

#### ↳ Re: Linkage Section variables

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-10-08T21:05:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8g9Hk.16831$h94.11185@fe12.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<d7abdb6a-42f1-4ae4-b409-b1d00eba3801@p31g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
If you try and reference (via the INITIALIZE or any other statement) a Linkage 
Section item that was NOT passed via a CALL statement *and* for which 
"addressability" has not otherwise been established (e.g. via SET statement), 
then results are UNPREDICTABLE.

Under many compilers an operating systems (depending on what else is going on in 
the system), you may cause your entire application to abnormally terminate. 
Otherwise, you may "trash" storage that belongs to another application or to the 
system itself.

This is NOT something to be done intentionally EVER
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Linkage Section variables

- **From:** Pakku <pakku@soccermail.com>

```
the called program via linkage section  These have been explicitly
defined in the WS of the calling program.  Since I am accessing
variables that have been passed and wouldn't it satisfy the criterion
of having addressability established.

But I get the impression that what is passed is not the individual
addresses of the 2 WS variables but just the address of the first
variable.

To clear up my example

In main program
X PIC X(4)
W PIC X(10)
Y PIC X(8)

MOVE 'ABCD' TO X
MOVE '2008-10-06' TO W
MOVE 'AUSERID' TO Y

CALL SUBPGM USING X, Y

Subprogram
LINKAGE SECTION
01 LS-VARS
05 P PIC X(4)
05 Q PIC X(8)

PROCEDURE DIVISION USING LS-VARS

When i step thru the code in the subprogram, I find that Q has the
value '2008-10-' in it rather than the anticipated 'AUSERID'.  (FWIW,
I retried this by declaring P and Q as 01 level variables and changed
the first stmt to say PROCEDURE DIVISION USING P Q with the same
result)

So, given that I am passing in variables that have been explicitly
declared in the calling program, why would it 'misbehave'?

This is a CICS program CALLing a statically linked subprogram on z/OS.

Thanks
```

## Thread: OT - Post Morem on OS/2 vs Windows??? — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help) · [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT - Post Morem on OS/2 vs Windows???

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-10-08T21:15:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Ip9Hk.433654$J72.413597@fe05.news.easynews.com>`

```
As someone who saw some (not much) of the "internal" IBM vs Microsoft inability 
to work together during the first two "joint" releases of OS/2 (while working on 
COBOL/2), I am curious if there are any GOOD "summary" sites (or books) that 
actually detail the demise of the relationship which (ultimately) led to the 
demise of OS/2?

The "simplistic" answer that I have often heard was that IBM thought that 
customers (their existing large corporate customers) would "settle" for a 
user-UNfriendly interface for several releases - to get the "structure" for this 
"multiprocessing scalable OS" working.  While Microsoft thought that their 
extensive (individual / "home" / "small business") customers cared MUCH more 
about the friendliness of the interface and were willing to "wait" for 
extended/enhanced OS capabilities.

Neither corporation would accept that the either management/marketing teams knew 
what they were talking about.

  ***

However, I would be interested in reading more "informed" post mortems.
```

#### ↳ Re: OT - Post Morem on OS/2 vs Windows???

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
> inability to work together during the first two "joint" releases of OS/2 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Really interesting post, Bill.

The sad thing is that they were BOTH right.

I was working for IBM (in the U.K.- some of the time at Hursley Park where 
CICS and the Spitfire were invented (not simultaneously...:-)) while OS/2 
was being distributed.

I remember it as a very good PC OS, and IBM gave us a free copy, as an 
employee perq. However, I reverted to Windows after I finished the contract. 
(Mainly because I needed to develop for Windows if I had any hope of selling 
the software - market forces, not necessarily technical superiority.)

If they could have settled their differences and managed to cater for both 
camps the whole history of modern computing could have been very different 
from what it is.


>  ***
>
> However, I would be interested in reading more "informed" post mortems.

For me, this is all now academic, but I agree it would be interesting to 
hear more insight from that time. Maybe lessons can be learned...

Pete.
```

```
The major US telephone company just rolled out a new methodology called Unified Process.

Testing. It has Integration, System and Regression testing, but no Unit. Why do you
suppose they omitted Unit? Answer: they don't trust programmers to test their own code.
```

```
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>>suppose they omitted Unit? Answer: they don't trust programmers to test their own code.

The FORMAT of a Unit Test document is similar to Integration and System Test: test cases,
expected and actual results. The difference in which planning document it links back to. A
Unit Test follows the Detailed Design; Integration is linked to high level design; System
is linked to Business Requirments.
```

## Thread: basic literature for Cobol — 3 msgs

### basic literature for Cobol

- **From:** jeksner <jeksner@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2008-10-09T12:44:01-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<85465e27-5fb8-47e0-af56-e68b6adc3ebb@k36g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi, can someone help me with list of literature for COBOL.

```

#### ↳ Re: basic literature for Cobol

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2008-10-09T21:58:59-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5qAHk.24787$i17.18622@newsfe07.iad>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<85465e27-5fb8-47e0-af56-e68b6adc3ebb@k36g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<85465e27-5fb8-47e0-af56-e68b6adc3ebb@k36g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
jeksner wrote:
> Hi, can someone help me with list of literature for COBOL.
> I am a beginner
> Thanks

Go googling on 'COBOL FAQ', 'COBOL books' etc., but rather than get 
overwhelmed getting a set of hard copies that are repetitive, and you 
don't really need, look for following, via Amazon.com or your local 
tecchie bookshop

  - Thane Hubbell, "Teach Yourself COBOL in 24 Hours".

Some years now since it was written, but still perfectly valid as a 
learning tool - BUT - use it as as a backup to the text or examples your 
instructor will give you.

As need arises you can google for COBOL sites that offer sample 
programs, usually universities or COBOL compiler manufacturer's sites. 
In my opinion the University of Limerick in Eire has a good basic set of 
examples :-

http://www.csis.ul.ie/COBOL/

Don't run before you can walk, and learn to digest the 'appetizers' 
first before you get on to the 'main course' (meat and potatoes); nor 
try and jump ahead of where your instructor is taking you - do your 
research in tandem with what he is currently covering.

"I am a beginner". A Newbie we call you. Totally new to IT and COBOL is 
your first shot, or do you have already a smattering of say Java or one 
of the "C" languages ?

Satisfy my curiosity a bit, because COBOL has gone down the tubes when 
it comes to teaching languages - (1) Why COBOL ?, and (2) Location - 
State/Province ?

Jimmy, Calgary AB

-----------------------------------------------------------------------

Note for PECD :

Computer clean-up; think I'm getting there after several attempts using 
Micro Trend Housecall and I downloaded (freebie) AVG V 8 - seems pretty 
comprehensive - ran for 1 hour 28 minutes against 622,591 0objects. The 
latter automatically triggered Windows Virus Check "ON". If I get the 
printer to print, (Uninstall and reinstall the Lexmark software), then 
I'll have a shot at resurrecting Net Express - more on this topic later.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: basic literature for Cobol

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-10-10T19:28:53+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6l8b16Fb62dqU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<85465e27-5fb8-47e0-af56-e68b6adc3ebb@k36g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <5qAHk.24787$i17.18622@newsfe07.iad>`

```


"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:5qAHk.24787$i17.18622@newsfe07.iad...
> jeksner wrote:
>> Hi, can someone help me with list of literature for COBOL.
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> comprehensive - ran for 1 hour 28 minutes against 622,591 0objects.

AVG is excellent. I have installed it for a number of friends and they are 
all OK with it. I think AVAST is actually better and Super Antispyware is 
best of the lot, but you don't get on-line protection from it unless you 
pay.

While trying to help someone else I picked up a couple of  Trojans and a 
Browser HiJack, which were detected by my system but managed to install 
themselves while it was distracted :-).  (The system sucessfully repelled 
three other simultaneous attacks...)

The Trojan signatures were not recognised by Norton or Trend but immediately 
spotted by Super AntiSpyware.

A HiJack This log showed no sign. (It is a very good tool and I have been 
glad of it in the past, however, this seems to be a new breed of malware.)

Fortunately, with IE7 you have much better control over add-ins than 
previously so I could disable them and get the Browser back fairly easily, 
but that didn't remove them. As long as the Browser was active they were 
communicating furiously (actually they were trying to; I had them 
blocked...) on two separate ports to remote web sites, until I disabled 
them.

Unfortunately, they used a very neat trick to close down the system if any 
attempt was made to remove them. (I won't give details here but it involved 
closing LSASS, which a Windows system simply won't tolerate. As this is what 
authenticates all users, it is probably right to refuse to run.)  Super 
AntiSpyware started trying to remove them but was unable to complete in the 
time before the system closed. They also prevented safe boot which was 
something I haven't seen before. I had to remove them manually by booting a 
special self contained, diagnostic version of Windows off a CD, deleting 
their .dlls, then scanning and cleaning the registry, and replacing the 
Winlogon.exe file which they had spoofed... All good stuff. It took me 4 
hours which I could ill afford at the moment.

Then I had to run some comprehensive scans to ensure the little buggers were 
gone... they were. A full scan of my system takes several hours as there are 
now around 1.6 million files on it :-)

It made me wonder what a normal person would do, if you simply didn't have 
the knowledge to fix these things. Take the machine to a computer shop where 
they would simply re-format, install Windows again then tell you to 
re-install all your applications. Not an option for me. I do have an image 
copy backup to a NAS drive usually once a week, with incremental backups of 
critical work I'm doing, but I would not want to rebuild this system...


>The latter automatically triggered Windows Virus Check "ON".

Not sure what you mean by this. There is no Windows Virus Check to my 
knowledge. Maybe you mean the Windows Firewall? If so, make sure it is OFF 
before installing AVG. Latest versions of AVG may run OK with it, earlier 
versions crashed and burned. Check the docs on AVG.

(There is a Windows Malware detection program that comes with automatic 
updates, but, as far as I know, you need to start it manually.)


If I get the
> printer to print, (Uninstall and reinstall the Lexmark software), then 
> I'll have a shot at resurrecting Net Express - more on this topic later.

If I were you, I'd make sure that all updates were applied, the system was 
clean and stable and run it for a couple of days until AVG was bedded down 
(and updating itself correctly), it was safe to turn Windows automatic 
updates back on (and they were functioning correctly) and only THEN try Net 
Express.

Anyway, good luck. Good to hear some positive progress.

Pete.
```
